Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Free Woman Contracts Manumission of Slave Husband
Ruling on a free woman offering payment for her slave husband's freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a free woman pays for her slave husband's manumission and explain why the marriage becomes fasid.

Core Idea
A free woman's payment for her slave husband's freedom corrupts the marriage because the consideration taints the contract.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario: a free woman instructs the master of her slave husband to free him in exchange for one thousand dirhams. The Hanafi ruling is that the marriage becomes corrupted (fasid). We also explore a refinement from the commentators: the master need not be a free adult; he can be a minor or even a slave acting as the woman's agent. This lesson clarifies the rationale behind the corruption and the scope of the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case involves a free woman, her slave husband, and his master. The woman asks the master to free her husband for a specified payment, and the master does so.
2
Determine the legal consequence
The marriage becomes fasid (corrupted). Understand that fasid in Hanafi law means the contract is defective but not automatically void; it requires rectification or separation.
3
Analyze the refinement about the master
The text refutes the condition that the master must be free or adult. Since the master acts as an agent (wakil) for the woman in manumission, his personal capacity does not affect the ruling.
4
Understand the rationale for corruption
The payment is effectively a consideration ('iwad) for the husband's freedom, which introduces an illicit condition into the marriage contract. Marriage in Islam cannot be made contingent on such financial exchanges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسد نكاح حرّة، قالت لسيد زوجها : أعتقه عني بألف ففعل

[٣] قوله: لسيد زوجها؛ قيَّده بعضهم بكونه حراً مكلفاً؛ ليمكن منه الاعتاق، ولا وجه له، فإن السيد في هذه الصورة لا يكون معتقاً، بل وكيلاً عن المرأة في الاعتاق، والبيع الثابت اقتضاء، فيمكن أن يكون السيد صبياً، أو رقيقاً: كأن يشتري العبد المأذون عبداً متزوجاً، أو يرثه الصبى، أو المجنون من أبيه، غاية الأمر أن يُتوقف بيع الصبي على إجازة الولي. كذا في حواشي «الدر المختار».

[٤] قوله: اعتقه؛ بصيغة الأمر من الاعتاق: أي اعتق زوجي عني بعوض ألف
And the marriage of a free woman is corrupted if she says to the master of her husband: 'Free him for me in exchange for a thousand,' and he does so.

[3] His saying: 'to the master of her husband'; some restricted it to the master being free and legally capable so that manumission is possible from him, but there is no basis for this, because in this scenario the master is not the manumitter, rather an agent for the woman in the manumission, and the sale is established by default. It is possible that the master is a minor or a slave: for example, if a ma'dhun slave purchases a married slave, or a minor or a lunatic inherits from his father. Ultimately, the sale of a minor is suspended upon the guardian's permission. As in the hawashi of al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

[4] His saying: 'free him'; in the imperative form from i'taq: meaning free my husband for me in exchange for a compensation of a thousand.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: if a free woman instructs the master of her slave husband to free him in exchange for one thousand dirhams, and the master does so, the marriage becomes corrupted (fasid). The commentary refutes the idea that the master must be a free adult; the master may be a minor or even a slave, because he acts as the woman's agent. The key is that the payment effectively becomes a consideration for the husband's freedom, which taints the marriage contract.

Key Terms
فسد النكاح (fasada al-nikah): The marriage contract becomes legally corrupted (fasid) due to an illicit condition or consideration.
سيد (sayyid): Master of a slave; the owner of the husband in this context.
عوض ('iwad): Compensation or consideration given in exchange for something, here the manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the marriage become corrupted in this case?
A) Because the woman paid money to a non-relative.
B) Because the payment taints the marriage contract with an illegal consideration.
C) Because the master must be a free adult.
D) Because the husband remained a slave.

Key Takeaway
A free woman who pays for her slave husband's manumission renders the marriage fasid, and this ruling applies regardless of the master's legal capacity because he acts as her agent.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Freeing a Husband by His Wife: Implied Ownership and Corruption of Marriage
Ruling on emancipation request implying sale and corruption of marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of iqtida' to determine that the marriage of a free woman to a slave is corrupted when she requests and procures his emancipation for a fee.

Core Idea
A wife's request to free her slave husband implies a prior purchase, making her his owner and corrupting the marriage.

Imagine a free woman married to a slave. She wants him freed and offers to pay the master. She asks: 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand.' The master does so. What happens to the marriage? Intuitively, the husband is freed, but the legal consequences are more complex. The Hanafi school rules that the marriage becomes corrupted because the wife becomes the owner of her husband through an implied sale. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and the intra-school debate with Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific scenario? A free woman (حرّة) married to a slave (تحت عبد) says to the slave's master: 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand.' The master complies. The ruling: the marriage becomes corrupted (فسد النكاح). Also, the emancipation is valid and the husband becomes free, but the wife becomes his owner.
2
Understand the legal problem
Why does the marriage become corrupted? Because the wife becomes the owner of her husband. A woman cannot be married to her own slave. So the marriage ends. But how does she become the owner? She did not explicitly buy him.
This is the key analytical step.
3
Trace the proof: the principle of iqtida'
The Hanafi school invokes iqtida' (implication). The wife's request to free him 'on my behalf' cannot be valid unless she owns him. Since emancipation of another's slave on one's behalf requires prior ownership, her speech implies that she first purchases him. Thus, the master's act is interpreted as: sell him to her, then free him on her behalf. Ownership is established by implication.
Iqtida' is defined as: دلالة اللفظ على مسكوت يتوقف عليه صدق الكلام أو صحته.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that the wife's request is nonsense (لغو) because she cannot ask to free what she does not own. The master's emancipation is on his own behalf, so no ownership transfers, and the marriage continues. Identify his proof and the point of disagreement.
Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and tarjih
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) respond that the request is not void; iqtida' establishes the necessary premise. Thus the sale is implied, ownership is created, and the marriage is corrupted. This is the mufta bihi position as affirmed by the commentator.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether the marriage ends immediately or continues.

The Book of Marriage / Chapter on the Marriage of Slaves and Disbelievers. And the marriage of a free woman is corrupted when she says to the master of her husband: 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand' and he does so. Meaning by the kinship of the son, for the slave girl is owned by the son, so the child follows her, and he becomes free upon his brother, because of his statement: 'Whoever owns a close relative who is prohibited in marriage, that person becomes free upon him.' ... (And the marriage of a free woman is corrupted when she says to the master of her husband: 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand' and he does so). A child becomes free because the child follows his mother in slavery and freedom. So if his mother is a slave girl of the son of the one who marries, the child becomes his slave. And a man, when he owns a close relative who is prohibited in marriage, becomes free upon him legally, so the child becomes free due to the kinship of the owner of his mother. [1] His statement: 'close relative'; al-rahim means kinship. And al-mahram is the one whose marriage is perpetually prohibited. These two conditions exclude when a man owns his cousin, for he does not become free, because although he is a close relative, he is not a prohibited degree. And when a man owns the mother of his wife, he does not become free because although the prohibition exists, kinship does not. This will be detailed in the Book of Emancipation, God willing. [2] His statement: 'emancipated' means the slave becomes free upon the owner. This hadith was narrated by al-Nasa'i from Ibn 'Umar, and by Ahmad and the authors of the Sunan from Samurah with the wording: 'Whoever owns a close relative who is prohibited in marriage, that person is free.' [3] His statement: 'to the master of her husband'; some restricted it to being free and legally capable so that emancipation is possible from him, but this is baseless, for the master in this scenario is not the emancipator, but rather an agent for the woman in the emancipation, and the sale is established by implication. So the master could be a boy or a slave, such as when a permitted slave buys a married slave, or a boy or a lunatic inherits from his father. At most, the sale of a boy is suspended on the permission of the guardian. Thus in the footnotes of al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [4] His statement: 'free him' is in the imperative form of emancipation, meaning: free my husband on my behalf for the compensation of a thousand. ... (A free woman married to a slave) says to the master of her husband: 'Free him for a thousand' and he does so. The command is valid, the husband becomes free upon his wife, and the marriage is corrupted, contrary to Zufar, for according to him he does not become free upon the woman due to the lack of ownership. And we say: by implication ownership is established, so it becomes as if she said: 'Sell him to me for such and such, then free him on my behalf,' and the master's saying 'I freed' becomes as if he said 'I sold him to you, then freed him on your behalf.' So when ownership is established by implication, the marriage is corrupted. Similarly the ruling if the husband of a slave girl says that to the guardian of his wife. As in al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'married to a slave' means she is married to a slave. Although this condition is not mentioned in the text, it is understood from the author's statement 'the master of her husband'. [2] His statement: 'upon his wife' means his emancipation is by way of agency, and the wife becomes his patroness, so the patronage belongs to her, not to him. [3] His statement: 'and the marriage is corrupted' because the husband becomes owned by the wife, and the marriage of an owned person to the owner is not valid, and if this occurs after marriage, it corrupts it. [4] His statement: 'contrary to Zufar'; he says that her saying 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand' is nonsense because it is a request for the emancipation of the master's slave by him on her behalf, which is impossible because there is no emancipation in what one does not own. So when her speech becomes invalid, the master's emancipation is for himself, not for her, so there is nothing that corrupts the marriage. [5] His statement: 'And we say ... etc.' is a response to Zufar's statement and an explanation of the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions. Its summary: When she ordered him to emancipate her husband on her behalf for a thousand, and it is known that emancipation is not possible in what she does not own, the sale is established by implication (iqtida'), which is the indication of the utterance to something unstated on which the truthfulness or validity of the speech depends. (1) Iqtida' is the indication of the utterance to something unstated on which the truthfulness or validity of the speech depends. The first type is like the hadith: 'The mistake and forgetfulness are forgiven' meaning the ruling of sin, for otherwise they occur in reality. The second type is like our issue, for it cannot be validated except by preceding ownership, since ownership is a condition for the validity of emancipation on her behalf, so ownership precedes by a sale as a matter of implication, and emancipation on the part of the one who commands is the implied matter. So when ownership is established for the one who commands, the marriage is corrupted due to the contradiction between the two matters. Then ownership in it is a condition, and conditions follow, so the implied sale is established with the conditions of the implied matter (emancipation), not with its own conditions, to show subordination. Its completion is in al-Bahr (3:221) and Radd al-Muhtar (2:385).
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqaya, discussing the ruling when a free woman married to a slave requests the slave's master to free him for a fee of one thousand. The master complies. The ruling has three components: the command is valid, the husband becomes free upon his wife (meaning the emancipation is performed on her behalf by agency), and the marriage is corrupted (fasid). The corruption occurs because the wife effectively becomes the owner of her husband through the implied sale (the fee is for the purchase, followed by the emancipation), and a woman cannot be married to her own slave. The commentator then addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar, who argued that the wife's request is legally meaningless because she does not own the slave, so the master's emancipation is on his own behalf, leaving the marriage intact. The Hanafi response is that the request necessarily implies a prior purchase (iqtida'), as it is impossible to free someone else's slave on another's behalf without first acquiring ownership. Thus, the master's act is interpreted as: first sell him to her, then free him on her behalf. This establishes ownership by implication, which invalidates the marriage. The commentary also clarifies that the master need not be free or legally capable, as he is merely an agent; if he is a minor or a slave himself, the sale is subject to guardian approval. The passage further explains the concept of iqtida' (implication) as a legal tool where an utterance necessitates an unstated premise for its validity. Here, the wife's request to free her husband presupposes her ownership, which is achieved by an implied sale. The same ruling applies analogously if the husband of a slave girl says similar words to his wife's guardian. This is a classic example of the Hanafi reliance on ma'qul al-ma'na (rational understanding) and istihsan (juristic preference) in legal reasoning.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): In this passage, iqtida' is defined as 'dilalat al-lafz 'ala maskut yatawaqqaf 'alayhi sidq al-kalam aw sihhatuh' (the indication of the utterance to something unstated on which the truthfulness or validity of the speech depends). Here, the wife's request to free the husband implies an unstated sale that establishes her ownership, which is a condition for the validity of the emancipation.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar al-Dabbi (d. 158/775) was a prominent early Hanafi jurist and a student of Abu Hanifa. He is known for his strict textualism and often differed from the majority of the school on issues involving qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position, what is the effect on the marriage when a free woman says to the master of her slave husband, 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand,' and the master does so?
A) The marriage remains valid because the master freed his own slave.
B) The marriage is corrupted because the wife becomes the owner of her husband.
C) The slave becomes free but the marriage continues.
D) The marriage becomes suspended pending the wife's payment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses iqtida' to establish an implied sale when a wife procures her slave husband's freedom for a fee, thereby making her his owner and corrupting the marriage.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
12 min
Free Woman's Request to Free Her Slave Husband: Implied Sale and Marriage Invalidity
Intra-Hanafi dispute with proof and objection responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of al-iqtida' and apply it to derive the ruling that a free woman becomes owner of her slave husband by implication when she requests his freeing for a price, leading to invalidation of the marriage.

Core Idea
A free woman's request to free her slave husband implies a prior sale, and once she owns him, the marriage is invalid.

In this passage, we examine a nuanced case where a free woman married to a slave asks the master to free her husband for a sum. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) rule that the marriage becomes invalid, while Zufar disagrees. The key is the legal principle of al-iqtida' (implication), where the wording implies what is necessary for its validity. This lesson dissects the dispute, the proof, and the responses to objections.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the marriage is invalid (fasid) when the woman requests freeing and the master complies.
The ruling is based on the implication of a prior sale.
2
Identify Zufar's opposing view
Zufar holds that the wife's request is meaningless because she does not own the husband, so the freeing is from the master for himself, and the marriage remains valid.
Zufar's position challenges the logical necessity of implication.
3
Apply the principle of al-iqtida'
Since freeing requires ownership, the request must imply a prior sale. Ownership is established by implication (muqtada), and the freeing becomes on her behalf.
This is the core of the Hanafi proof.
4
Address the first objection
The objection that one person cannot perform both sides of a sale is countered: the master's statement 'I freed' is not a separate offer; it confirms the implied sale, and the acceptance is inherent in the wife's original command.
The sale is established by implication, not by formal offer and acceptance.
5
Address the second objection
The objection that ownership by implication is limited to necessity is countered: once ownership is established for freeing, it is complete ownership, and marriage automatically becomes invalid due to conflict of status.
The necessity extends to the effect of ownership on marriage.

أي حُرَّة تحت عبد) قالت لسيّدِ زوجها أعتقه بألف ، ففعل صح الأمر، ويعتق الزوج على امرأته " ، ويفسد النكاح" خلافاً لزفر ، فإنه لا يعتق على المرأة عنده ؛ لعدم الملك. ونحن نقول : بالاقتضاء ) يثبتُ الملك ، فصار كما لو قالت : بعْهُ مِنِّي بكذا ، ثُمَّ أعتقهُ عنِّي ، وقول المولى : أعتقت صارَ كما لو قال بعتُه منك ، ثُمَّ أعتقه عنك، فلما ثبت الملك اقتضاء ، فسد النكاح. وكذا الحكم إذا قال ذلك زوج الأمة لولي زوجته. كما في «البحر». [١]قوله : تحت عبد ؛ أي متزوجة بعبد ، وهذا القيد وإن لم يكن مذكوراً في المتن ، لكنَّه فهم من قول المصنف «السيد زوجها». [٢] قوله : على امرأته ؛ يعني يكون عتقه عنها بطريق الوكالة، وتكون الزوجة مولاة له ؛ ولهذا يكون الولاء لها لا له. (۳) قوله : ويفسد النكاح ؛ لكون الزوج مملوكاً للزوجة، ولا يصح نكاح المملوك بالسيدة، ولو طرأ ذلك بعد النكاح يفسده. [٤] قوله : خلافاً لزفر له ؛ هو يقول إن قولها : اعتقه عنِّي بألف، لغو؛ لأنه طلب لعتق المأمور عبده عنه ، وهو محالٌ إذ لا عتق فيما لا يملك ، فلما بطل قولها كان عتق السيد عن نفسه لا عنها ، فلا يوجد ما يفسد النكاح. [0] قوله : ونحن نقول ... الخ ؛ جواب عن قول زفر ، وتوجيه لقول الإمام وصاحبيه ، وحاصله : إنه لَمَّا أمرته بإعتاق زوجها عنها بألف ، ومن المعلوم أن العتق لا يمكن فيما لا تملكه ، ثبت البيع بالاقتضاء، وهو دلالة اللفظ على مسكوت يتوقف عليه صدق الكلام، أو صحته. (۱) اقتضاء : هو دلالة اللفظ على مسكوت يتوقف عليه صدق الكلام أو صحته ، فالأول : كحديث : (رفع الخطأ والنسيان)، أي رفع حكمهما وهو الإثم وإلا فهما واقعان في الخارج، والثاني كمسألتنا فإنه لا يمكن تصحيحه إلا بتقديم الملك ، إذ الملك شرط لصحة العتق عنه فتقدم الملك بالبيع مقتضى، والإعتاق عن الآمر مقتض بالكسر ، فإذا ثبت الملك للأمر فسد النكاح للتنافي بين الأمرين، ثم الملك فيه شرط والشروط أتباع فلذا ثبت البيع المقتضى بشروط المقتضي وهو العتق لا بشروط نفسه إظهارا للتبعية، وتمامه في البحر (۳) : (۲۲۱) ، و(رد المحتار»(٢ : ٣٨٥). ويَرِدُ عليه أن غاية ما في الباب أنه صارَ كقوله : بـع عبدك منى بألف ، فقال الآخر : بعت. لا ينعقد البيع ؛ لأنَّ الواحد لا يتولى طرفي البيع بخلاف النكاح. وأيضاً" الملك الذي يثبت بطريق الاقتضاء ملك ضروري، فيثبت بقدر الضرورة، ولا ضرورة في ثبوته في حق النكاح حتَّى يفسد النكاح. فإنه لا يمكن تصحيح أمره بالاعتاق عنها إلا بثبوت ملكها فيه ؛ لكون الملك شرطاً لصحة العتق ، فكأنها قالت لسيد زوجها : بع عبدك - وهو زوجها - منّي بألف ثمّ أعتقه عنِّى ، ولَمَّا ثبت ملكها فسد نكاحها. [١] قوله : ويرد عليه ... الخ ؛ حاصلُ الإيراد الأول : إنا سلّمنا أن قولها : أعتقه عني بألف كقولها : بعه مني بألف ثمّ أعتقه عنّي ، وقوله : «أعتقت» كقوله : «بعت منك ثم أعتقت عنك»، لكن هذا القدر لا يفيد لثبوت الملك حتى يتفرع عليه فساد النكاح ، وذلك لما تقرر في مقره ، وقد سبق نُبَدٌ من تفصيله : إن الواحد لا يتولى طرفي البيع : أي الإيجاب والقبول بخلاف النكاح، فإنه يتولى طرفيه واحد. وكذا صرح في «الهداية» وغيرها : إن البيع لا ينعقد بلفظين أحدهما لفظ الأمر كأن يقول أحدهما بعه منى بكذا ، ويقول الآخر : بعتُ فلا ينعقد البيع ما لم يقل الآخر قبلت أو اشتريت، ولو قال لوليها : زوّجني ابنتك ، فقال : زوجتك. ينعقد النكاح ومن المعلوم أن فيما نحن فيه وإن جعل قوله : «اعتقت» بمنزلة قوله : «بعت منك ثم أعتقت»، لكن لم يوجد القبولُ بعده من جانبها، وإنما الموجود من جانبها هو قولها بعه مني، وهو لا يدلُّ على إنشاء البيع في الحال ؛ ليكون قبولاً، بل هو أمر به، وتوكيل وإذا لم يوجد القبول لم يصح البيعُ لفوت أحد ركنيه، وهو الإيجاب والقبول. (٢) قوله : وأيضاً ... الخ ؛ هذا إيراد آخر على التوجيه المذكور، وحاصله : أنه قد تقرر في مقره أن ما يثبت بالاقتضاء يكون ضرورياً ؛ لثبوته بغرض صدق الكلام، أو صحته، وما يكون ضرورياً يتقدَّرُ بقدر الضرورة، ولا يتعدى إلى ما عداها ؛ ولهذا صرحوا بأن لا عموم للمقتضي بخلاف الملفوظ حقيقة أو حكماً : كالمحذوف فإنّه يتصفُ بالعموم والخصوص كليهما.
i.e. a free woman under (married to) a slave) said to the master of her husband: 'Free him for a thousand,' so he did; the command is valid, and the husband is freed on behalf of his wife, and the marriage becomes invalid, contrary to Zufar, for it is not freed on behalf of the woman according to him due to lack of ownership. And we say: by implication (al-iqtida'), ownership is established, so it is as if she said: 'Sell him to me for such-and-such, then free him on my behalf,' and the master's saying 'I have freed' becomes as if he said 'I sold him to you, then freed him on your behalf.' When ownership is established by implication, the marriage is invalidated. Likewise the ruling if the husband of a slave woman said that to the guardian of his wife. As in 'al-Bahr'. [1] His saying: 'under a slave' means married to a slave, and this condition, although not mentioned in the text, is understood from the author's saying 'the master of her husband.' [2] His saying: 'on behalf of his wife' means his freeing is by way of agency, and the wife becomes his patron; therefore the wala' belongs to her, not to him. (3) His saying: 'and the marriage becomes invalid' because the husband becomes a slave owned by the wife, and marriage of a slave to his mistress is not valid, and if that occurs after marriage it invalidates it. [4] His saying: 'contrary to Zufar' - he says that her saying 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand' is nonsense, because it is a request to free the commanded person's slave on her behalf, which is impossible since there is no freeing in what one does not own. When her statement becomes invalid, the master's freeing is for himself, not for her, so there is nothing that invalidates the marriage. [0] His saying: 'And we say... etc.' is a response to Zufar's statement and an explanation of the Imam and his two companions' statement. Its summary: When she commanded him to free her husband for a thousand, and it is known that freeing is not possible in what she does not own, a sale is established by implication, which is the indication of the wording to an unspoken matter upon which the truthfulness or validity of the speech depends. (1) Al-iqtida': It is the indication of the wording to an unspoken matter upon which the truthfulness or validity of the speech depends. The first type: like the hadith 'The error and forgetfulness are lifted' i.e. their ruling is lifted, which is sin, otherwise they occur externally. The second type is like our issue, for it cannot be validated except by preposing ownership, since ownership is a condition for the validity of freeing on behalf of another. So ownership is preposed (established first) through a sale by implication (muqtada), and the freeing on behalf of the commander is the demanding (muqtadi) in the sense of requiring. Then when ownership is established for the commander (the wife), the marriage is invalidated due to the contradiction between the two matters (ownership and marriage). Then ownership in it is a condition, and conditions are subordinate, so the implied sale is established by the conditions of the demanding (the freeing) and not by its own conditions, to show subordination. Its completion is in 'al-Bahr' (3): (221) and 'Radd al-Muhtar' (2:385). And an objection is raised against it: The upshot is that it becomes like his saying: 'Sell your slave to me for a thousand,' and the other says: 'I sold.' The sale is not concluded because one person cannot undertake both sides of a sale, contrary to marriage. Also, the ownership established by way of implication is necessary ownership, so it is established to the extent of necessity, and there is no necessity for its establishment in the case of marriage such that it invalidates the marriage. For it is not possible to validate the matter of freeing on her behalf except by her ownership being established in him, because ownership is a condition for the validity of freeing. So it is as if she said to the master of her husband: 'Sell your slave - who is her husband - to me for a thousand, then free him on my behalf.' When her ownership is established, her marriage is invalidated. [1] His saying: 'And an objection is raised against it... etc.' Summary of the first objection: We concede that her saying 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand' is like her saying 'Sell him to me for a thousand then free him on my behalf,' and his saying 'I freed' is like his saying 'I sold to you then freed on your behalf.' But this amount does not help to establish ownership such that the invalidity of marriage branches from it, because of what is established in its place, and a portion of its detail has preceded: That one person cannot undertake both sides of a sale, i.e. offer and acceptance, contrary to marriage, for one person undertakes both sides of it. Likewise it is stated in 'al-Hidayah' and others that a sale is not concluded with two expressions where one is a command, like one saying 'Sell it to me for such-and-such' and the other says 'I sold' - the sale is not concluded as long as the other does not say 'I accept' or 'I buy.' But if he said to her guardian: 'Marry me to your daughter,' and he said: 'I marry you,' the marriage is concluded. And it is known that in our case, although his saying 'I freed' is treated as his saying 'I sold to you then freed,' yet no acceptance from her side exists after it. What exists from her side is her saying 'Sell to me,' which does not indicate the creation of the sale at the moment to be an acceptance; rather it is a command and appointment. And if acceptance does not exist, the sale is not valid due to the loss of one of its pillars, which is offer and acceptance. (2) His saying: 'Also... etc.' This is another objection to the mentioned explanation. Its summary: It has been established in its place that what is proven by implication is necessary, because it is established for the purpose of truthfulness or validity of speech, and what is necessary is measured by the extent of the necessity, and does not extend beyond it. Therefore they explicitly stated that there is no generality for the implied meaning, unlike the uttered word, in reality or virtually like the omitted word, for it is characterized by both generality and specificity.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a classical Hanafi fiqh discussion on the ruling when a free woman married to a slave asks the master to free her husband for a sum of money. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute (with Zufar) accompanied by proof and response to objections. The matn states the ruling: If the command is obeyed, the husband is freed on behalf of the wife, and the marriage becomes invalid (فسد النكاح). Zufar disagreed, arguing that the wife does not own the husband, so her request is meaningless and the master frees his own slave, leaving the marriage intact. The Hanafi response (attributed to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani) invokes the principle of al-iqtida' (implication). Since freeing requires ownership, her request must imply a prior sale to establish ownership. Thus, the master's act of freeing is treated as if he said, 'I sold him to you, then freed him on your behalf.' Once ownership is established by implication, the marriage becomes invalid because a woman cannot be married to her own slave. The text then addresses two objections to this reasoning. First objection: In a sale, one person cannot act as both buyer and seller (offer and acceptance cannot be from the same person in a sale, unlike marriage). In our case, the master's statement 'I freed' is treated as both offer and acceptance, which would be invalid. The response is that the master's statement is not a sale; it is a freeing that implies a prior sale, and the acceptance from the wife is implicit in her original request. But the objection argues that her request is a command, not an acceptance, so the sale is incomplete. The second objection: Ownership by implication is limited to the necessity that requires it. Since the necessity is only for freeing, it should not extend to invalidate the marriage. The response is that once ownership is established for the purpose of freeing, it is complete ownership, and the marriage automatically becomes invalid due to the conflict of status. The passage demonstrates how Hanafi jurists use rational inference (al-iqtida') to derive rulings from the apparent meaning of speech.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): The indication of wording to an unspoken matter upon which the truthfulness or validity of the speech depends, e.g., the phrase 'Free him on my behalf' implies a sale to give ownership, otherwise the freeing would be impossible.
مقتضى (muqtada): The implied matter or requirement; here, the prior sale of the slave to the wife.
مقتضي (muqtadi): The demanding or requiring speech; here, the wife's statement requiring the freeing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a free woman married to a slave asks the master to free the husband for a price, and the master does so, what is the Hanafi ruling on the marriage according to the majority?
A) The marriage remains valid because the wife did not own the husband.
B) The marriage becomes invalid because the husband becomes the wife's property.
C) The marriage is suspended until the price is paid.
D) The marriage is valid only if the wife pays the price from her own wealth.

Key Takeaway
A free woman's command to free her slave husband for a price implies a prior sale, and resulting ownership invalidates the marriage.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Two Objections to the Implied Sale Ruling
Presents two objections to the implied sale ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two objections that challenge the Hanafi view that an implied sale from freeing a slave husband does not invalidate the marriage.

Core Idea
Two objections expose why an implied sale might seem to invalidate marriage: acceptance is missing and ownership is only necessary.

The matn suggested that when a woman says to her husband's master 'Free him from me for a thousand' and the master frees him, an implied sale occurs that gives her ownership, thus invalidating the marriage. But the commentator raises two powerful objections. In this lesson, we explore these objections and why they appear compelling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage and locate the two objections introduced by the commentator. The first concerns the lack of acceptance in the sale; the second concerns the limited scope of necessary ownership.
2
Understand the first objection: missing acceptance
Analyze why a normal sale requires both offer and acceptance. In this case, the woman only gave a command ('Sell to me'), and the master said 'I freed,' which is like saying 'I sold.' But without the woman's acceptance, the sale would be incomplete. Unlike marriage, where one person can handle both sides, sale requires separate offer and acceptance.
Reference to al-Hidayah: sale with command and response 'I sold' is not valid without 'I accept.'
3
Understand the second objection: limited necessary ownership
Even if a sale is implied, ownership established by implication (iqtida') is limited to necessity. The only necessity here is to validate the freeing, not to affect the marriage. Hence, the marriage should not be invalidated.
Implication (muqtada) has no generality; it is confined to the purpose.
4
Summarize the two objections
Restate: (1) Sale requires acceptance, which is missing. (2) Implied ownership is necessary only for freeing, not for marriage.

ويَرِدُ عليه أن غاية ما في الباب أنه صارَ كقوله : بـع عبدك منى بألف ، فقال الآخر : بعت. لا ينعقد البيع ؛ لأنَّ الواحد لا يتولى طرفي البيع بخلاف النكاح. وأيضاً" الملك الذي يثبت بطريق الاقتضاء ملك ضروري، فيثبت بقدر الضرورة، ولا ضرورة في ثبوته في حق النكاح حتَّى يفسد النكاح. فإنه لا يمكن تصحيح أمره بالاعتاق عنها إلا بثبوت ملكها فيه ؛ لكون الملك شرطاً لصحة العتق ، فكأنها قالت لسيد زوجها : بع عبدك - وهو زوجها - منّي بألف ثمّ أعتقه عنِّى ، ولَمَّا ثبت ملكها فسد نكاحها. [١] قوله : ويرد عليه ... الخ ؛ حاصلُ الإيراد الأول : إنا سلّمنا أن قولها : أعتقه عني بألف كقولها : بعه مني بألف ثمّ أعتقه عنّي ، وقوله : «أعتقت» كقوله : «بعت منك ثم أعتقت عنك»، لكن هذا القدر لا يفيد لثبوت الملك حتى يتفرع عليه فساد النكاح ، وذلك لما تقرر في مقره ، وقد سبق نُبَدٌ من تفصيله : إن الواحد لا يتولى طرفي البيع : أي الإيجاب والقبول بخلاف النكاح، فإنه يتولى طرفيه واحد. وكذا صرح في «الهداية» وغيرها : إن البيع لا ينعقد بلفظين أحدهما لفظ الأمر كأن يقول أحدهما بعه منى بكذا ، ويقول الآخر : بعتُ فلا ينعقد البيع ما لم يقل الآخر قبلت أو اشتريت، ولو قال لوليها : زوّجني ابنتك ، فقال : زوجتك. ينعقد النكاح ومن المعلوم أن فيما نحن فيه وإن جعل قوله : «اعتقت» بمنزلة قوله : «بعت منك ثم أعتقت»، لكن لم يوجد القبولُ بعده من جانبها، وإنما الموجود من جانبها هو قولها بعه مني، وهو لا يدلُّ على إنشاء البيع في الحال ؛ ليكون قبولاً، بل هو أمر به، وتوكيل وإذا لم يوجد القبول لم يصح البيعُ لفوت أحد ركنيه، وهو الإيجاب والقبول. (٢) قوله : وأيضاً ... الخ ؛ هذا إيراد آخر على التوجيه المذكور، وحاصله : أنه قد تقرر في مقره أن ما يثبت بالاقتضاء يكون ضرورياً ؛ لثبوته بغرض صدق الكلام، أو صحته، وما يكون ضرورياً يتقدَّرُ بقدر الضرورة، ولا يتعدى إلى ما عداها ؛ ولهذا صرحوا بأن لا عموم للمقتضي بخلاف الملفوظ حقيقة أو حكماً : كالمحذوف فإنّه يتصفُ بالعموم والخصوص كليهما. والجواب عن الأول : إن البيع الثَّابت بالاقتضاء مستغن عن القبول، فإنَّه قد عُرف في أصول الفقه [؟] أن المقتضى [؟] ليس كالملفوظ ، بل هو أمر ضروري فيسقط من الأركان والشروط مما يحتمل السقوط. إذا تمهد هذا، فنقول : الملك الثابت فيما نحن فيه بطريق البيع إنما هو اقتضاء اعتبر لتصحيح قولها : اعتقه عنّي بألف» ؛ لظهور عدم إمكان عتق عبد الغـيـر عـن غـيـر المالك، فيجب أن يثبت بقدر الضرورة، هو تصحيح الكلام، ولا يثبت من كلِّ وجه حتى يلزم منه فسادُ النكاح. [١] قوله : إن البيع ; حاصله إن قولها : اعتقه عني بألف»، وإن كان كقولها : بعه مني بألف ، ثم أعتقه لكن من المعلوم أن البيع الثابت هاهنا ليس ملفوظاً لا حقيقةً ولا حكماً، بل هو ثابت اقتضاء ، والبيعُ الثابت اقتضاء يستغني عن القبول. وبالجملة لو قال أحد لمالك العبد : بع عبدك مني بألف ، فقال المالك : بعت، لا ينعقد البيع ما لم يقل الأول قبلْتُ لفوات القبول، وأما هاهنا فثبوت البيع بطريقِ الاقتضاء، كما تثبت الشروط اقتضاء؛ لتصحيح الكلام ومـ ومثله ينعقد بدون القبول، وإنما تعتبر فيه شروط المقتضى - بالكسر - لا شروط نفسه وأركانه إظهاراً للتبعية. فتشترط أهلية الآمر للاعتاق حتى لو كان صبياً مأذوناً لم يثبت البيع، ويسقط القبول الذي هو ركن البيع ولا يثبت فيه خيار رؤية، أو عيب، ولا يشترط كونه مقدور التسليم، فيصح الأمر بإعتاق العبد الآبق. كذا في «البحر». (۲) [٢] قوله : إن المقتضى ; بصيغة المجهول : أي الذي يثبت اقتضاء ، قال الشارح في «التنقيح» و«التوضيح»: أما المقتضى فنحو اعتق عبدك عني بألف يقتضي البيع ضرورة صحة العتق ، فصار كأنه قال : «بع عبدك مني بألف ، وكُن وكيلي في الاعتاق» فيثبت أي البيعُ بقدر الضرورة، ولا يكون كالملفوظ حتى لا تثبت شروطه : أي لا يجب أن يثبت جميع شروطه، بل يثبت من الأركان والشروط ما لا يحتمل السقوط أصلاً ، لكن ما يحتمل السقوط في الجملة لا يثبت.
And it is objected against it that the extent of the matter in this chapter is that it becomes like his statement: 'Sell your slave to me for a thousand,' and the other said: 'I sold.' The sale is not concluded because one person does not handle both sides of the sale, unlike marriage. And also: Ownership that is established by way of implication (iqtida') is necessary ownership, so it is established to the extent of necessity, and there is no necessity for its establishment concerning marriage such that the marriage is invalidated. For it is not possible to validate the matter of freeing him from her except by establishing her ownership in him; because ownership is a condition for the validity of freeing. So it is as if she said to her husband's master: 'Sell your slave—and he is her husband—to me for a thousand, then free him for me.' And when her ownership is established, her marriage becomes invalid. [1] His statement: 'And it is objected...' etc.; the gist of the first objection: We concede that her statement: 'Free him for me for a thousand' is like her statement: 'Sell him to me for a thousand then free him for me,' and his statement: 'I freed' is like his statement: 'I sold from you then I freed for you,' but this amount does not benefit for establishing ownership so that marrige invalidation branches upon it. That is because it is established in its place, and some details have preceded: that one person does not handle both sides of the sale, i.e., offer and acceptance, unlike marriage, for in marriage one person handles both sides. Likewise, it is stated in al-Hidayah and others: sale is not concluded with two utterances where one is a command, like one says: 'Sell it to me for such,' and the other says: 'I sold,' so the sale is not concluded unless the other says: 'I accept' or 'I bought.' And if he said to her guardian: 'Marry me to your daughter,' and he said: 'I married you,' the marriage is concluded. And it is known that in our case, even if his statement: 'I freed' is treated as his statement: 'I sold from you then I freed,' there is no acceptance from her side after it. Rather, what exists from her side is her statement: 'Sell to me,' which does not indicate the creation of a sale immediately to be an acceptance; rather, it is a command and agency. And when acceptance is absent, the sale is not valid due to the missing of one of its pillars, namely offer and acceptance. (2) His statement: 'And also...' etc.; this is another objection to the aforementioned interpretation. Its gist: It has been established in its place that what is established by implication (iqtida') is necessary; because it is established for the purpose of truthfulness of speech or its validity, and what is necessary is measured according to necessity and does not extend beyond it. For this reason they stated that there is no generality for the implied (muqtada) unlike the uttered explicitly or by legal fiction (hukman) like the deleted word, for it is characterized by both generality and particularity. And the answer to the first: That the sale established by implication (iqtida') is independent of acceptance. For it is known in usul al-fiqh that the implied (muqtada) is not like the uttered; rather it is a necessary matter, so it falls from the pillars and conditions what can possibly fall. When this is established, we say: The ownership established in our case by way of sale is only an implication considered to validate her statement: 'Free him for me for a thousand'; due to the apparent impossibility of freeing another's slave on behalf of a non-owner, so it must be established to the extent of necessity, which is the validation of speech, and it is not established from every aspect such that it would necessitate the invalidation of the marriage. [1] His statement: 'Indeed the sale...'; its gist: That her statement: 'Free him for me for a thousand,' even if it is like her statement: 'Sell him to me for a thousand then free him,' it is known that the sale established here is not uttered neither in reality nor by legal fiction; rather it is established by implication (iqtida'), and the sale established by implication is independent of acceptance. In short, if someone said to the master of a slave: 'Sell your slave to me for a thousand,' and the master said: 'I sold,' the sale is not concluded unless the first says 'I accepted' due to the absence of acceptance. But here, the sale is established by way of implication, just as conditions are established by implication for the validation of speech, and the like is concluded without acceptance. Rather, in it the conditions of the implied (muqtada) are considered, not the conditions and pillars of its own, as a manifestation of subordination. So the legal capacity of the commander for freeing is required; even if he were a minor permitted to trade, the sale is not established. And acceptance, which is a pillar of sale, is dropped, and there is no option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) or defect (khiyar al-'ayb) in it, and it is not required that the object be deliverable, so the command to free an absconding slave is valid. Thus in al-Bahr. (2) [2] His statement: 'Indeed the implied (al-muqtada)'; in the passive voice: meaning that which is established by implication. The commentator said in al-Tanqih and al-Tawdih: As for the implied (muqtada), it is like 'Free your slave for me for a thousand' which implies the sale due to the necessity of the validity of freeing, so it becomes as if he said: 'Sell your slave to me for a thousand and be my agent in freeing.' So the sale is established to the extent of necessity, and it is not like the uttered such that its conditions are not established: i.e., it is not obligatory that all its conditions be established; rather, from the pillars and conditions what cannot possibly fall at all are established, but what can possibly fall in general is not established.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a ruling from an earlier matn (likely al-Wiqayah). The issue is: if a woman says to her husband's master 'Free him from me for a thousand' and the master says 'I have freed him,' does this imply a sale that would give the woman ownership of her husband and thus invalidate the marriage? The matn had suggested that the marriage is invalidated. The commentator presents two objections and their answers.

**First objection:** The woman's statement 'Free him from me for a thousand' is analogous to 'Sell him to me for a thousand' and the master's 'I have freed' is analogous to 'I have sold to you.' But in a sale, one person cannot handle both offer and acceptance; the buyer must say 'I accept' after the seller's offer. Here, the woman only gave a command, not an acceptance. Thus there is no valid sale, so no ownership transfers, and the marriage should remain valid. This is supported by a reference to al-Hidayah: a sale with a command like 'Sell to me' and response 'I sold' is not concluded without explicit acceptance, unlike marriage where the same person can act for both parties.

**Second objection:** Even if a sale is implied (iqtida') from the woman's words, ownership established by implication is limited to necessity. Here, the necessity is only to validate the freeing; there is no necessity for ownership in the context of marriage. So the implied ownership does not extend to invalidating the marriage.

**Answer to the first objection:** A sale established by implication (iqtida') is not like an explicit sale; it is a necessary legal construct. As known in usul al-fiqh, the implied (muqtada) is not uttered and does not require all the pillars and conditions of a normal sale. Specifically, it does not require acceptance. The sale is established solely to validate the woman's command to free; it is independent of acceptance.

**Answer to the second objection:** The ownership established by implication is measured by necessity. The necessity here is only to make the freeing valid, which requires temporary ownership. That ownership is limited to the purpose of freeing and does not create full ownership that affects the marriage. The marriage remains valid.

**Further elucidation from al-Bahr and al-Tanqih:** The commentator explains that the sale by implication is not uttered; it is a constructive sale. As such, it has its own conditions: the commander must have legal capacity for freeing (e.g., must be sane, adult; if a minor permitted to trade, the sale is not implied). Acceptance is dropped. Options like option of inspection or defect do not apply. The object need not be present or deliverable; thus commanding to free an absconding slave is valid. The key principle is that the implied (muqtada) is not like the uttered; only those pillars and conditions that absolutely cannot be dropped are established; others that can possibly be dropped are not established.

Thus, the ruling of the Hanafi madhhab is that the marriage is not invalidated by such a transaction. The woman becomes owner of her husband only to the extent necessary for freeing, and that limited ownership does not invalidate the marriage. This is the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtidā’): Implication or necessary consequence in legal interpretation where a term implies something else for the validity of speech.
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): That which is implied or established by necessity (iqtida'), such as the sale implied from the command to free.
ملك ضروري (milk ḍarūrī): Ownership established out of necessity, limited to the extent required to validate a legal act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against the ruling that the marriage is invalidated?
A) The woman did not pay the money.
B) The sale lacks acceptance from the woman.
C) The master did not own the slave.
D) The freeing was conditional.

Key Takeaway
Two objections challenge the invalidation of marriage: lack of acceptance in the sale and the limited scope of necessary ownership.

The Hanafi Answers: Why Implied Sale Has Limited Effect
Provides the Hanafi responses to the two objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi responses to the two objections and derive that the marriage remains valid.

Core Idea
Sale by implication (iqtida') drops the need for acceptance and limits ownership to necessity — marriage is not invalidated.

The two objections seemed to undermine the matn's ruling, but the Hanafi jurists provide a principled defense. They show that a sale established by implication is not like an explicit sale; it is a necessary construct that requires only the minimal conditions. In this lesson, we unpack these answers and see why the marriage remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two objections
Quickly review the two objections from the previous lesson: (1) lack of acceptance, (2) necessary ownership limited.
2
Examine the first answer: implied sale does not require acceptance
The answer explains that a sale by implication (iqtida') is not like a regular sale. It is a necessary legal construct to validate speech. As such, it does not need all pillars, especially acceptance. The sale is implied solely for the purpose of making the freeing valid, so acceptance is dropped.
Reference to usul al-fiqh: the implied is not like the uttered.
3
Examine the second answer: necessary ownership limited
The ownership established by implication is measured by necessity. Only enough ownership to validate the freeing is created. It does not extend to invalidating the marriage because there is no necessity for that.
The implied (muqtada) has no generality; it is confined to the necessity.
4
Understand additional details: conditions and dropped features
Further, the implied sale has its own conditions: the commander must have legal capacity (e.g., not a minor). Other features of a regular sale, like option of inspection or defect, are also dropped because they are not necessary.
From al-Bahr: acceptance is dropped, object need not be deliverable.
5
Conclude: the marriage remains valid
Because the implied sale does not create full ownership, the marriage is not affected. Thus the mufta bihi is that the marriage remains valid.

(٢) قوله : وأيضاً ... الخ ؛ هذا إيراد آخر على التوجيه المذكور، وحاصله : أنه قد تقرر في مقره أن ما يثبت بالاقتضاء يكون ضرورياً ؛ لثبوته بغرض صدق الكلام، أو صحته، وما يكون ضرورياً يتقدَّرُ بقدر الضرورة، ولا يتعدى إلى ما عداها ؛ ولهذا صرحوا بأن لا عموم للمقتضي بخلاف الملفوظ حقيقة أو حكماً : كالمحذوف فإنّه يتصفُ بالعموم والخصوص كليهما. والجواب عن الأول : إن البيع الثَّابت بالاقتضاء مستغن عن القبول، فإنَّه قد عُرف في أصول الفقه [؟] أن المقتضى [؟] ليس كالملفوظ ، بل هو أمر ضروري فيسقط من الأركان والشروط مما يحتمل السقوط. إذا تمهد هذا، فنقول : الملك الثابت فيما نحن فيه بطريق البيع إنما هو اقتضاء اعتبر لتصحيح قولها : اعتقه عنّي بألف» ؛ لظهور عدم إمكان عتق عبد الغـيـر عـن غـيـر المالك، فيجب أن يثبت بقدر الضرورة، هو تصحيح الكلام، ولا يثبت من كلِّ وجه حتى يلزم منه فسادُ النكاح. [١] قوله : إن البيع ; حاصله إن قولها : اعتقه عني بألف»، وإن كان كقولها : بعه مني بألف ، ثم أعتقه لكن من المعلوم أن البيع الثابت هاهنا ليس ملفوظاً لا حقيقةً ولا حكماً، بل هو ثابت اقتضاء ، والبيعُ الثابت اقتضاء يستغني عن القبول. وبالجملة لو قال أحد لمالك العبد : بع عبدك مني بألف ، فقال المالك : بعت، لا ينعقد البيع ما لم يقل الأول قبلْتُ لفوات القبول، وأما هاهنا فثبوت البيع بطريقِ الاقتضاء، كما تثبت الشروط اقتضاء؛ لتصحيح الكلام ومـ ومثله ينعقد بدون القبول، وإنما تعتبر فيه شروط المقتضى - بالكسر - لا شروط نفسه وأركانه إظهاراً للتبعية. فتشترط أهلية الآمر للاعتاق حتى لو كان صبياً مأذوناً لم يثبت البيع، ويسقط القبول الذي هو ركن البيع ولا يثبت فيه خيار رؤية، أو عيب، ولا يشترط كونه مقدور التسليم، فيصح الأمر بإعتاق العبد الآبق. كذا في «البحر». (۲) [٢] قوله : إن المقتضى ; بصيغة المجهول : أي الذي يثبت اقتضاء ، قال الشارح في «التنقيح» و«التوضيح»: أما المقتضى فنحو اعتق عبدك عني بألف يقتضي البيع ضرورة صحة العتق ، فصار كأنه قال : «بع عبدك مني بألف ، وكُن وكيلي في الاعتاق» فيثبت أي البيعُ بقدر الضرورة، ولا يكون كالملفوظ حتى لا تثبت شروطه : أي لا يجب أن يثبت جميع شروطه، بل يثبت من الأركان والشروط ما لا يحتمل السقوط أصلاً ، لكن ما يحتمل السقوط في الجملة لا يثبت.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a ruling from an earlier matn (likely al-Wiqayah). The issue is: if a woman says to her husband's master 'Free him from me for a thousand' and the master says 'I have freed him,' does this imply a sale that would give the woman ownership of her husband and thus invalidate the marriage? The matn had suggested that the marriage is invalidated. The commentator presents two objections and their answers.

**First objection:** The woman's statement 'Free him from me for a thousand' is analogous to 'Sell him to me for a thousand' and the master's 'I have freed' is analogous to 'I have sold to you.' But in a sale, one person cannot handle both offer and acceptance; the buyer must say 'I accept' after the seller's offer. Here, the woman only gave a command, not an acceptance. Thus there is no valid sale, so no ownership transfers, and the marriage should remain valid. This is supported by a reference to al-Hidayah: a sale with a command like 'Sell to me' and response 'I sold' is not concluded without explicit acceptance, unlike marriage where the same person can act for both parties.

**Second objection:** Even if a sale is implied (iqtida') from the woman's words, ownership established by implication is limited to necessity. Here, the necessity is only to validate the freeing; there is no necessity for ownership in the context of marriage. So the implied ownership does not extend to invalidating the marriage.

**Answer to the first objection:** A sale established by implication (iqtida') is not like an explicit sale; it is a necessary legal construct. As known in usul al-fiqh, the implied (muqtada) is not uttered and does not require all the pillars and conditions of a normal sale. Specifically, it does not require acceptance. The sale is established solely to validate the woman's command to free; it is independent of acceptance.

**Answer to the second objection:** The ownership established by implication is measured by necessity. The necessity here is only to make the freeing valid, which requires temporary ownership. That ownership is limited to the purpose of freeing and does not create full ownership that affects the marriage. The marriage remains valid.

**Further elucidation from al-Bahr and al-Tanqih:** The commentator explains that the sale by implication is not uttered; it is a constructive sale. As such, it has its own conditions: the commander must have legal capacity for freeing (e.g., must be sane, adult; if a minor permitted to trade, the sale is not implied). Acceptance is dropped. Options like option of inspection or defect do not apply. The object need not be present or deliverable; thus commanding to free an absconding slave is valid. The key principle is that the implied (muqtada) is not like the uttered; only those pillars and conditions that absolutely cannot be dropped are established; others that can possibly be dropped are not established.

Thus, the ruling of the Hanafi madhhab is that the marriage is not invalidated by such a transaction. The woman becomes owner of her husband only to the extent necessary for freeing, and that limited ownership does not invalidate the marriage. This is the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtidā’): Implication or necessary consequence in legal interpretation where a term implies something else for the validity of speech.
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): That which is implied or established by necessity (iqtida'), such as the sale implied from the command to free.
ملك ضروري (milk ḍarūrī): Ownership established out of necessity, limited to the extent required to validate a legal act.

A) Because the woman's command itself serves as acceptance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the marriage remains valid because the implied sale is limited by necessity and does not create full ownership.

Sale by Iqtida: Independence from Acceptance
Addresses the first objection regarding the lack of acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the sale by iqtida does not require acceptance and identify which conditions and pillars are dropped.

Core Idea
The sale by iqtida is a necessary inference, not an uttered contract, so only indispensable pillars and conditions are retained.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a statement implies a transaction out of necessity, the law recognizes it as a valid contract to the extent needed. This lesson examines the first objection raised against the wife's statement 'Free him for me for a thousand'—that a sale normally requires acceptance. The commentary explains how the concept of iqtida resolves this by dropping acceptance and non-essential conditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify that the wife did not explicitly say 'I sell' and 'I buy', so the apparent lack of acceptance would normally invalidate the sale.
The objection assumes a normal sale; the answer relies on the special nature of iqtida.
2
Grasp the concept of iqtida
Learn that in usul al-fiqh, al-muqtaḍā (that which is established by necessity) is not like an explicit utterance. It is a necessary inference to validate speech, so only essential elements remain.
The sale is inferred from the command to free for a price.
3
Identify which pillars and conditions are dropped
Note that acceptance (qabūl) is dropped as a pillar, and conditions like option of sight or defect and ability to deliver are not required. Only the eligibility of the commander for freeing is considered.
This shows the limited scope of the inferred contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول : إن البيع الثَّابت بالاقتضاء مستغن عن القبول، فإنَّه قد عُرف في أصول الفقه أن المقتضى ليس كالملفوظ ، بل هو أمر ضروري فيسقط من الأركان والشروط مما يحتمل السقوط. إذا تمهد هذا، فنقول : الملك الثابت فيما نحن فيه بطريق البيع إنما هو اقتضاء اعتبر لتصحيح قولها : اعتقه عنّي بألف» ؛ لظهور عدم إمكان عتق عبد الغـيـر عـن غـيـر المالك، فيجب أن يثبت بقدر الضرورة، هو تصحيح الكلام، ولا يثبت من كلِّ وجه حتى يلزم منه فسادُ النكاح. قوله : إن البيع ؛ حاصله إن قولها : اعتقه عني بألف»، وإن كان كقولها : بعه مني بألف ، ثم أعتقه لكن من المعلوم أن البيع الثابت هاهنا ليس ملفوظاً لا حقيقةً ولا حكماً، بل هو ثابت اقتضاء ، والبيعُ الثابت اقتضاء يستغني عن القبول. وبالجملة لو قال أحد لمالك العبد : بع عبدك مني بألف ، فقال المالك : بعت، لا ينعقد البيع ما لم يقل الأول قبلْتُ لفوات القبول، وأما هاهنا فثبوت البيع بطريقِ الاقتضاء، كما تثبت الشروط اقتضاء؛ لتصحيح الكلام ومثله ينعقد بدون القبول، وإنما تعتبر فيه شروط المقتضى - بالكسر - لا شروط نفسه وأركانه إظهاراً للتبعية. فتشترط أهلية الآمر للاعتاق حتى لو كان صبياً مأذوناً لم يثبت البيع، ويسقط القبول الذي هو ركن البيع ولا يثبت فيه خيار رؤية، أو عيب، ولا يشترط كونه مقدور التسليم، فيصح الأمر بإعتاق العبد الآبق. كذا في «البحر». قوله : إن المقتضى ؛ بصيغة المجهول : أي الذي يثبت اقتضاء ، قال الشارح في «التنقيح» و«التوضيح»: أما المقتضى فنحو اعتق عبدك عني بألف يقتضي البيع ضرورة صحة العتق ، فصار كأنه قال : «بع عبدك مني بألف ، وكُن وكيلي في الاعتاق» فيثبت أي البيعُ بقدر الضرورة، ولا يكون كالملفوظ حتى لا تثبت شروطه : أي لا يجب أن يثبت جميع شروطه، بل يثبت من الأركان والشروط ما لا يحتمل السقوط أصلاً ، لكن ما يحتمل السقوط في الجملة لا يثبت. وعن الثاني: إن الثابت بالاقتضاء ، وإن كان ضرورياً يثبت به لوازمه التي لا يحتمل السقوط، كما سيأتي في مسألة الهبة: إن الهبة الاقتضائية لا بد لها من القبض، فبطلان ملكِ النكاح من لوازم ثبوتِ ملك اليمين بحيث لا ينفك عنه. ( والولاء لها ) ؛ لأنه عتق عليها ، ويقع عن كفارتها لو نوت به) : أي نوت بهذا الإعتاق الاعتاق عن الكفارة يقعُ عن الكفارة ، (وإن قالت ذلك بلا بدل لم يفسد ، والولاء له) : أي للسيد ، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وكذا عند محمد.
And the answer to the first [objection]: Indeed, the sale established by iqtida (necessity/inference) is independent of acceptance. For it is known in usul al-fiqh that that which is established by iqtida is not like the uttered [word]; rather it is a necessary matter, so what can be omitted from the pillars and conditions is dropped. When this is established, we say: The ownership established in our case by way of sale is only by iqtida, considered to validate her statement: 'Free him for me for a thousand'; because of the apparent impossibility of freeing another's slave on behalf of someone other than the owner. So it must be established to the extent of necessity, which is to validate the speech, and it is not established in every respect such that it would necessitate the invalidity of the marriage. [Commentator's] statement: 'Indeed the sale' – its summary: Her statement 'Free him for me for a thousand', although it is like her saying 'Sell him to me for a thousand, then free him', it is known that the sale established here is not uttered, neither in reality nor in effect; rather it is established by iqtida, and the sale established by iqtida is independent of acceptance. In short, if someone said to the owner of a slave: 'Sell me your slave for a thousand', and the owner said: 'I sold', the sale is not concluded unless the first says 'I accepted' due to the missing acceptance. But here, the establishment of the sale is by way of iqtida, just as conditions are established by iqtida to validate the speech, and its like is concluded without acceptance. Rather, in it are considered the conditions of that which necessitates [the action] (al-muqtaḍī) – with kasra – not its own conditions and pillars, to show its subsidiary nature. So the eligibility of the commander for freeing is required, such that if he were an authorized minor, the sale would not be established. And acceptance, which is a pillar of sale, is dropped, and no option of sight or defect is established in it, nor is it required that it be under ability to deliver, so the command to free an escaped slave is valid. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. [Commentator's] statement: 'Indeed al-muqtaḍā' – in the passive voice: i.e., that which is established by iqtida. The commentator said in 'al-Tanqih' and 'al-Tawdih': As for al-muqtaḍā, it is like 'Free your slave from me for a thousand' which necessitates the sale necessarily for the validity of the freeing, so it becomes as if he said: 'Sell me your slave for a thousand and be my agent in freeing.' So the sale is established to the extent of necessity, and it is not like the uttered [word] so that its conditions are not established: i.e., it is not required that all its conditions be established; rather, from the pillars and conditions only that which cannot be dropped at all is established, but what can be dropped in general is not established. And regarding the second [objection]: Indeed, that which is established by iqtida, even if it is necessary, its necessary consequences that cannot be dropped are established, as will come in the issue of gift: The gift by iqtida must have possession, so the invalidity of marriage ownership is among the necessary consequences of the establishment of ownership of the slave such that it cannot be separated from it. 'And the wala' is for her' because the freeing is on her behalf. 'And it counts toward her expiation if she intended it' – i.e., if she intended with this freeing the expiation freeing, it counts toward the expiation. 'And if she said that without a price, it does not become invalid, and the wala' is for him' – i.e., for the master. And this according to Abu Hanifa is for him, and likewise according to Muhammad.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the legal ruling concerning a wife's statement to her husband's master: 'Free him for me for a thousand.' The author addresses two objections. The first objection is that a sale normally requires an offer (ījāb) and acceptance (qabūl). The answer is that the sale here is established by necessity (iqtidā') rather than by explicit utterance. In uṣūl al-fiqh, something established by necessity is not like an explicit statement; it only carries what is necessary. Thus, the sale is established to the extent needed to validate the freeing, which is the intention of the speaker. The acceptance (qabūl) is dropped as a pillar of sale, and conditions like option of sight or defect are not required. The only condition considered is the eligibility of the commander (the wife) for freeing. The second objection is that if the sale is established, its necessary consequence would be that the wife owns the slave, and ownership of a spouse is invalid for marriage. The answer is that the sale established by iqtidā' only establishes necessary consequences that cannot be dropped. The invalidity of the marriage (due to ownership) is a necessary consequence of full ownership, but here ownership is only partial and limited to the extent needed for the freeing. Therefore, the marriage does not become invalid. The passage then concludes with rulings on the effects: the wala' (right of inheritance by emancipation) goes to the wife because the freeing is on her behalf. If she intended this freeing as her expiation (kaffāra), it counts. If she said this without mentioning a price, the freeing does not become invalid and the wala' goes to the master (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; Abu Yusuf may have a different view, but the relied-upon position is that the wala' is for the master in the case without price.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtidā'): A legal inference of a transaction by necessity to validate speech or action, without explicit utterance.
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): That which is established by iqtidā', i.e., the inferred contract or ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is dropped as a pillar in a sale by iqtida?
A) Offer (ījāb)
B) Acceptance (qabūl)
C) Price (thaman)
D) Seller's eligibility

Key Takeaway
The inferred sale by iqtida drops acceptance and non-essential conditions, retaining only what is necessary to validate the speech.

Effects of Sale by Iqtida: Wala', Kaffara, and the Dispute Without Price
Discusses the effects of sale by iqtida and the dispute over wala' in the case without price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the rulings on wala' and kaffara in a sale by iqtida and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute when no price is given.

Core Idea
In sale by iqtida, the wala' follows the actual emancipator; if no price is given, the wala' returns to the master according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

The second objection in the text concerns the effect of the inferred sale on the marriage. After resolving that, the passage details the consequences: the wala' belongs to the wife if she pays a price, and the freeing counts toward her expiation. An intra-Hanafi difference arises when no price is mentioned: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the wala' goes to the master, while Abu Yusuf may differ.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
The objection is that if the sale by iqtida establishes ownership, then the wife would own her husband, making the marriage invalid.
This is a serious consequence; the answer lies in the limited nature of iqtida ownership.
2
Identify necessary consequences of iqtida
The text explains that what is established by necessity only includes consequences that cannot be dropped. The invalidity of marriage is not a necessary consequence here because ownership is partial.
Compare to the example of gift by iqtida requiring possession.
3
Apply the wala' rule and note the dispute
When a price is given, the wala' goes to the wife because she is the actual emancipator. If no price, the freeing is still valid but the wala' goes to the master (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Understand the practical difference.
The hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' is key.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā'): The right of inheritance and loyalty that transfers to the emancipator from the freed slave.
الكفارة (al-kaffāra): An expiatory act, such as freeing a slave, to atone for certain sins.
