Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Free Woman Commanding Slave Husband's Master to Free Him
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wife's command to free slave husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on this issue, explain the reasoning based on iqtida' and the distinction between sale and gift, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
With a price, ownership by implied sale invalidates the marriage; without a price, the marriage stands because a gift requires possession.

This lesson explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about a free woman married to a slave. She commands the master to free him, either with or without specifying a price. The legal consequences—whether she becomes the owner, the marriage is invalidated, and who receives wala'—depend on how we characterize the implied transaction. We will follow the commentary's analysis and the arguments between the three great Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish the case where the wife says 'Free him for me for a thousand' (with a price) from the case where she says 'Free him for me' (without a price). Note that the legal outcome differs according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
With a price: implied sale → ownership for wife → marriage invalidated → wala' for wife → valid for expiation. Without a price: no ownership via sale nor gift (since possession missing) → marriage valid → wala' for master.
3
State the ruling of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf treats both cases equally: in both, ownership is established via implied gift, possession requirement is dropped by necessity, marriage invalidated, wala' for wife.
4
Analyze the key distinction: sale vs. gift
Sale requires offer and acceptance; acceptance can be dropped by iqtida'. Gift requires possession; possession can never be dropped. Abu Yusuf's analogy fails because possession is a condition that cannot be waived.
This is the core of the dispute.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentary upholds Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, since possession in gift is indispensable. Therefore, without a price, no ownership transfer, marriage remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الرقيق والكافر

والولاء لها، ويقع عن كفارتها لو نوت به، وإن قالت ذلك بلا بدل لم يفسد، والولاء له.

وعن الثاني: إن الثابت بالاقتضاء، وإن كان ضرورياً يثبت به لوازمه التي لا يحتمل السقوط، كما سيأتي في مسألة الهبة: إن الهبة الاقتضائية لا بد لها من القبض، فبطلان ملكِ النكاح من لوازم ثبوتِ ملك اليمين بحيث لا ينفك عنه. ( والولاء لها )؛ لأنه عتق عليها، ويقع عن كفارتها لو نوت به: أي نوت بهذا الإعتاق الاعتاق عن الكفارة يقعُ عن الكفارة، (وإن قالت ذلك بلا بدل لم يفسد، والولاء له): أي للسيد، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة لله، وكذا عند محمد. [1] قوله: وعن الثاني ... الخ؛ حاصله: إن كون الثابت اقتضاء ضرورياً ثابتاً بقدر الضرورة غير متعدّياً عن مواضع الضرورة مسَلَّمٌ، لكن لا مناص من ثبوت لوازمه التي لا تنفك عنه، ومن المعلوم أن بطلان النكاح لازم لثبوت ملك اليمين لزوما لا انفكاك بينهما، فلا بد أن يثبت ذلك بثبوته. [2] قوله: في مسألة الهبة؛ أي في المسألة التي تأتي عن قريب: وهي ما إذا قالت لسيد زوجها: «اعتقه عنِّي» من غير ذكر العوض. [3] قوله: والولاء لها؛ يعني في الصورة المذكورة إذا فعل المولى حسبما أمرته وقع العتق عنها، وكان ولاء العتق لها؛ لأن ولاء العتاقة - وهو بالفتح - يستحقه المعتق من المعتق بالفتح - بسبب إعتاقه، لا يمكن أن يكون لغير المعتق؛ لحديث: «الولاء لِمَن أعتق» أخرجه الشيخان وغيرهما بألفاظ مختلفة، والمعتق في هذه الصورة إنما هو الزوجة وسيد الزوج إنما هو وكيلها في الاعتاق فيكون الولاء لها لا له. (4) قوله: ويقع ... الخ؛ حاصله إنه لو كان عليها عتق رقبة في كفارة من الكفارات التي يجب فيها عتق الرقبة لكفارة اليمين ونوت بهذا الاعتاق الذي فعله سيد زوجها بعدما أمرته بقولها: «اعتقه عني بألف» أداء كفارتها صح ذلك. [0]قوله: وإن قالت ... الخ؛ يعني إن قالت زوجة العبد الحرّة لسيد زوجها: اعتقه عني ولم تذكر عوضاً الألف أو غيره، وفعل ذلك مولاه لا يقع العتق عنها بل

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية

وأما عند أبي يوسف الله فهذا والأوَّلُ سواء"، فيثبت الملك "" هنا بطريق الهبة، وتستغني " الهبة عن القبض، وهو شرط كما يستغنى البيع عن القبول، وهو ركن. عنه، فنقول: القَبول ركن يحتمل السقوط، فيكون الولاء له، ولا يفسد نكاحها لعدم وجود ما ينافي ملك النكاح، وهو ملك اليمين.

[1]قوله: فهذا والأول سواء؛ أي عدم ذكر البدل، وذكره عنده سواء في أنه يقعُ العتق في كليهما عن الآمرة، ويكون الولاء لها، ويفسد نكاحها. [2] قوله: فيثبت الملك؛ يعني قولها: اعتقه عنّي بألف»، كما كان مفيداً لملكها بطريق البيع اقتضاء يكون قولها: اعتقه عني»، مفيداً لملكها بطريق الهبة اقتضاء. والسر فيه: أن تصحيح كلام العاقل واجب مهما أمكن، وقد أمكن هذا باقتضاء الملك هبة كما أمكن سابقاً باقتضاء الملكِ بيعاً، وإذا ثبت ملكها وقع العتق عنها وفسد نكاحها. [3] قوله: وتستغنى ... الخ؛ دفعُ دَخل مقدر، تقرير الدخل: أن الهبة لا تتمّ ولا تفيد ملك الموهوب له الموهوب إلا بقبضه الموهوب على ما سيأتي تفصيله في موضعه. وهاهنا وإن أمكن جعل قولها قائماً مقام: هبة لي، ثم اعتقه عنِّي، وقول السيد: أعتقته عنك، قائماً مقام: وهبته لك ثمّ أعتقتُه عنك، لكن لا يثبت منه الملك لانتفاء قبض الآمرة ذلك الموهوب، فتكون الهبة بدونه غير مفيدة للملك. وحاصل الدفع: أن القبض شرط من الهبة والقبول ركن في البيع، فلما أمكن سقوط القبول في الصورة السابقة، وتم البيع بدونه أمكن سقوط القبض في الهبة هاهنا، فتتم الهبة، وتفيد الملك وإن فُقد القبض. [4]قوله: فنقول؛ هذا جواب عن استدلال أبي يوسف وتأييد لمذهبهما، وحاصله: أنه قد مر أن الثابت اقتضاء يثبت ضرورةً ويسقط فيه من شروطه وأركانه ما يحتمل السقوط لا ما لا يحتمل السقوط، والقبض في الهبة لا يسقط أبداً، فلا توجد هبة تتم بدونه شرعاً، فلا يسقط هاهنا أيضاً بخلاف البيع، فإنَّ القبول قد يسقط فيه.
The Book of Marriage, Chapter on the Marriage of Slaves and Disbelievers. And the patronage (wala') is for her, and it counts for her expiation if she intended it. And if she said that without a price, it is not invalidated, and the patronage is for him. And regarding the second: what is established by implication, even if necessary, establishes its necessary consequences that cannot be dropped, as will come in the issue of gift: the implied gift necessarily requires possession. So the nullification of the marriage is a consequence of the establishment of ownership by right hand such that it is inseparable. And the patronage is for her; because it is manumission on her behalf, and it counts for her expiation if she intended it: meaning she intended by this manumission to free for expiation, it counts for expiation. (And if she said that without a price it is not invalidated, and the patronage is for him): meaning for the master, and this according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and likewise according to Muhammad. [1] His statement: 'And regarding the second... etc.'; its summary: that what is established by implication is established necessarily to the extent of necessity, not extending beyond the places of necessity, is conceded, but there is no escape from establishing its necessary consequences that are inseparable from it. And it is known that the nullification of marriage is a necessary consequence of establishing ownership by right hand, an inseparable consequence, so it must be established by its establishment. [2] His statement: 'In the issue of gift'; meaning in the issue that will come shortly: which is when she says to the master of her husband: 'Free him for me' without mentioning compensation. [3] His statement: 'And the patronage is for her'; meaning in the mentioned case, if the master does as she ordered, the manumission occurs on her behalf, and the patronage of manumission is for her; because the patronage of manumission – with fatha on the 'ayn – is entitled to the manumitter from the manumitted due to his manumission, it cannot be for other than the manumitter; due to the hadith: 'The patronage is for the one who manumits,' narrated by the two Shaykhs and others with different wordings, and the manumitter in this case is the wife, and the master of the husband is only her agent in manumission, so the patronage is for her, not for him. (4) His statement: 'And it counts... etc.'; its summary: that if she owed the manumission of a slave for an expiation among the expiations that require manumission of a slave, like the expiation for a vow, and she intended by this manumission which the master of her husband did after she ordered him by saying 'Free him for me for a thousand' to fulfill her expiation, that is valid. [0] His statement: 'And if she said... etc.'; meaning if the free wife of the slave said to the master of her husband: 'Free him for me' without mentioning compensation, whether the thousand or other, and the master did that, the manumission does not occur on her behalf but rather... 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya. And as for Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, this and the first are the same; so ownership is established here by way of gift, and the gift dispenses with possession, which is a condition, just as sale dispenses with acceptance, which is a pillar. So we say: acceptance is a pillar that can be dropped, so patronage is for him, and her marriage is not invalidated due to the absence of what contradicts the ownership of marriage, which is ownership by right hand. [1] His statement: 'So this and the first are the same'; meaning not mentioning the price and mentioning it are the same in his view in that the manumission occurs in both cases on behalf of the commandress, and the patronage is for her, and her marriage is invalidated. [2] His statement: 'So ownership is established'; meaning her saying 'Free him for me for a thousand' was beneficial for her ownership by way of implied sale, so her saying 'Free him for me' is beneficial for her ownership by way of implied gift. The secret is: that it is obligatory to correct the speech of a rational person as much as possible, and this has been possible by implying ownership by gift just as was possible previously by implying ownership by sale. And if her ownership is established, the manumission occurs on her behalf and her marriage is invalidated. [3] His statement: 'And it dispenses... etc.'; a rebuttal of an implied objection. The presentation of the objection: the gift is not completed and does not give the donee ownership of the gifted item except by his possession of the gifted item, as will come in its place. And here, although it is possible to make her saying stand in place of 'Give it to me as a gift, then free him for me,' and the master's saying 'I freed him for you' stand in place of 'I gave him to you as a gift then I freed him for you,' ownership is not established due to the lack of the commandress's possession of that gifted item, so the gift without it does not give ownership. The summary of the rebuttal: that possession is a condition for the gift and acceptance is a pillar in sale. Since it was possible to drop acceptance in the previous case and the sale was completed without it, it is possible to drop possession in the gift here, so the gift is completed and gives ownership even though possession is lost. [4] His statement: 'So we say'; this is a response to Abu Yusuf's argument and support for their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) madhhab. Its summary: that it has passed that what is established by implication is established necessarily and its conditions and pillars that can be dropped are dropped, not those that cannot be dropped. And possession in the gift can never be dropped, so there is no gift that is completed without it legally, so it is not dropped here either, unlike sale, for acceptance can be dropped in it.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya deals with a specific issue in the chapter on marriage of slaves and disbelievers. The commentary expands on the base text (likely from al-Hidayah) concerning a free woman married to a slave. She commands the master of her slave husband to free him. The discussion examines whether this command, with or without a specified price, results in the woman owning her husband, thereby invalidating her marriage (since a woman cannot own her husband), and to whom the patronage (wala') belongs. The passage presents the views of the Hanafi imams. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: if the woman says 'Free him for me for a thousand,' it implies a sale, she becomes owner, the marriage is invalidated, wala' is for her, and it counts for her expiation if she intends. If she says it without a price, it does not invalidate the marriage, and wala' remains with the master. Abu Yusuf disagrees: in both cases the marriage is invalidated, wala' is for her, regardless of mentioning a price. The commentary then justifies Abu Hanifa's position by arguing that what is established by implication (اقتضاء) only imposes necessary consequences that are inseparable. The nullification of marriage is a necessary consequence of ownership by right hand, so it applies when ownership is established (as in the case with a price). Without a price, ownership is not established via sale but could be via gift; however, gift requires possession (قبض) which is not present here. Abu Yusuf analogizes to sale where acceptance (قبول) can be dropped, arguing that possession in gift can similarly be dropped. The commentary refutes this: acceptance is a pillar that can sometimes be dropped due to necessity, but possession in gift can never be dropped according to the school. Therefore, Abu Yusuf's analogy fails, and the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is upheld.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Legal implication; deriving a contract or ownership from words that do not expressly state it, based on necessity and context.
ولاء (wala'): Patronage or legal connection between the manumitter and the freed slave, granting inheritance rights and responsibility.
هبة (hibah): Gift; a transfer of ownership without compensation, requiring offer, acceptance, and possession (qabd) for completion.
قبض (qabd): Possession; physical or constructive taking of the gifted item, required for the gift to be complete and ownership to transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a free woman says to the master of her slave husband: 'Free him for me' without mentioning any price, what is the ruling regarding her marriage?
A) The marriage is invalidated and wala' is for the woman.
B) The marriage remains valid and wala' is for the master.
C) The marriage is invalidated but wala' is for the master.
D) The marriage remains valid and wala' is for the woman.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a marriage when a wife commands the freeing of her slave husband hinges on whether a price is mentioned, due to the distinct requirements of implied sale versus implied gift.

The Hanafi Principle of Ghayr Mukhatabin in Marriages of Disbelievers
Ruling on marriages without witnesses and during iddah after conversion, with talil and ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that disbelievers are not addressed with Islamic subsidiary laws and apply it to determine the validity of marriages without witnesses and during iddah after conversion.

Core Idea
Disbelievers are not addressed with Islamic subsidiary laws; their marriages remain valid after conversion if valid by their own religion.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi principle: disbelievers are not held accountable for Islamic legal rulings during their state of disbelief. When they convert, past marriages contracted according to their own permissible practices are upheld, even if those same contracts would be invalid if performed by Muslims. The source passage addresses two specific scenarios: marriage without witnesses and marriage during a waiting period (iddah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios and ruling
Find the two scenarios mentioned: marriage without witnesses and marriage during iddah. Note that the ruling for both (according to Abu Hanifa) is that the spouses are 'confirmed upon it' (أقرا عليه) after conversion.
The ruling applies only if they believed the marriage was valid in their religion.
2
Extract the legal reasoning (talil)
The text states the reason: 'they are not addressed during their state of disbelief with the rulings of Islam' (غير مخاطبين). Also, 'we are commanded to leave them with what they practice' (أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون). Identify these as the core proofs.
This is a principle, not a specific textual dalil, but it is derived from Quranic commands like 'to you your religion, to me mine'.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on iddah
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argued that marriage during iddah should be annulled because its prohibition is a matter of consensus across religions. The author responds that the consensus is among Muslims, not binding on disbelievers at the time of contract.
The author rejects the distinction between lack of witnesses (disputed) and iddah (consensus) because both Islamic rules are irrelevant to disbelievers.
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider a case where a disbeliever marries his sister (mahram) in a religion that allows it, then converts. Use the principle to predict the ruling (covered in next lesson).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الرقيق والكافر فإن أسلم المتزوجان بلا شهود أو في عدة كافر معتقدين ذلك، أقرا عليه ، وإن أسلم الزوجان المحرّمان فُرِّق بينهما ، والطفل مسلم [٢]قوله : فإن أسلم ؛ شروع في مباحث أنكحة الكفّار، وحاصله : إن الكافر إذا تزوج بكافرة بدون سماع الشهود، أو تزوج امرأة كانت معتدة من كافر طلقها، أو مات عنها، وكان ذلك جائزاً عندهم، ثم أسلم الزوجان أبقيا على نكاحهما ؛ وذلك لأن كل نكاح حرام بين المسلمين لفقد شرط يجوز في حقهم إذا اعتقدوا جوازه ، ويقرون عليه بعد الإسلام ؛ لأنا أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون. والسر فيه : إنّهم لا يخاطبون حالة كفرهم بحقوق الإسلام، فإذا نكحوا بطريق جائز عندهم تم ذلك النكاح ، ويبقى ذلك بعد الإسلام أيضاً. وفي مسألة العدّة خلاف لأبي يوسف ومحمد ، هما يقولان : إن حرمة نكاح معتدة الغير مجمع على حرمته، وحرمة النكاح بلا شهود مختلف فيه عندنا، ونحن نقول : لا أثر لهذا الفرق ، فإن الكفّارَ غير مخاطبين حالة كفرهم لا بهذا ولا بهذا. [٣) قوله : وإن أسلم ؛ أي إن نكح كافر محرماً من المحارم، ثم أسلما يفرق بينهما لا لبطلان النكاح في الكفر، فإنه عند المجوس جائز ، بل لأن المحرمية تنافي بقاء النكاح بخلاف ما مر ، فإن الشهادة لا تشترط حالة البقاء، وكذا العدة لا تنافيه. [٤]قوله : والطفل ... الخ ؛ يعني إن كان أحد الأبوين مسلماً، والآخر كافراً بأن (۱) (الهداية (٦) : ٢٥٢).
Book of Marriage, Chapter on the Marriage of the Slave and the Disbeliever. If the two spouses who married without witnesses, or during a waiting period (iddah) of a disbeliever, believing that to be permissible, they are confirmed upon (conversion). And if the two spouses who are in a prohibited relationship (mahram) embrace Islam, they are separated. The child (is) Muslim. [2] His statement: 'if they embrace Islam' is an entry into the topics of the marriages of disbelievers. Its gist: that if a disbeliever marries a disbelieving woman without the hearing of witnesses, or marries a woman who was in iddah from a disbeliever who divorced her or died from her, and that was permissible according to them, then the two spouses embrace Islam, they are kept on their marriage. That is because every marriage that is forbidden among Muslims due to the lack of a condition is permissible in their case if they believe in its permissibility, and they are confirmed upon it after Islam, because we are commanded to leave them with what they practice. The reason: they are not addressed during their state of disbelief with the rulings of Islam. So if they marry in a way permissible in their religion, that marriage is complete and remains after Islam as well. On the issue of iddah, there is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: they say that the prohibition of marrying a woman in the iddah of another is a matter of consensus, while the prohibition of marriage without witnesses is disputed among us. But we say: this distinction has no effect, because the disbelievers are not addressed during their disbelief with either of these. [3] His statement: 'and if they embrace Islam' means that if a disbeliever marries a mahram (prohibited relative) and then both embrace Islam, they are separated, not because the marriage is invalid in disbelief, for it is permissible among the Magians, but because the relationship of mahram conflicts with the continuation of the marriage, unlike what preceded, for witnesses are not required for the continuation, and likewise iddah does not conflict with it. [4] His statement: 'and the child' means that if one of the parents is Muslim and the other is a disbeliever, then... (ref: al-Hidayah 6:252)
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on marriages of disbelievers addresses three scenarios: marriage without witnesses, marriage during iddah, and marriage to a mahram. The general rule is that marriages contracted in disbelief are upheld after conversion to Islam if they were considered valid by the couple's religion and they acted in good faith. The legal reasoning (سر) is that disbelievers are not addressed by Islamic rulings during their disbelief; therefore, their past marriage is judged by their own law. For marriage without witnesses: it is upheld because the lack of witnesses does not affect the continuation of the marriage. For marriage during iddah: there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that marriage to a woman in iddah is prohibited by consensus (even among earlier religions), so it should be invalid after Islam. But the author rejects this distinction: since disbelievers are not addressed by Islamic laws at the time, the consensus among Muslims is irrelevant to them. For marriage to a mahram: it is not automatically invalid in disbelief (Magians allow it), but after Islam, the couple must be separated because the prohibition arising from lineage persists and is incompatible with marriage continuation. The child's ruling: if one parent is Muslim at the time of birth, the child is Muslim. The passage relies on the principle that disbelievers are not addressed (غير مخاطبين) with subsidiary rulings (furu') but are addressed with fundamental beliefs (usul al-iman).

Key Terms
غير مخاطبين (ghayru mukhaṭabīn): Not addressed with the rulings; meaning that disbelievers are not held accountable for Islamic legal rulings (furu') during their state of disbelief.
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the distinction made by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad between marriage without witnesses and marriage during iddah?
A) Because both are equally prohibited in Islam
B) Because disbelievers are not addressed with either ruling during their disbelief
C) Because marriage without witnesses is also a consensus
D) Because the couple converted after the marriage

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers' marriages, if valid in their own religion, remain valid after conversion to Islam, because they were not held accountable for Islamic subsidiary laws at the time of marriage.

Rulings on Mahram Marriage and Child’s Religion after Conversion
Ruling on mahram marriage annulment and child's religion after conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why marriage to a mahram is annulled upon conversion and determine a child's religion based on the status of parents at the time of birth.

Core Idea
Marriage to a mahram, though valid in disbelief, is annulled upon conversion because the prohibition is perpetual and irreconcilable with marriage continuity.

This lesson examines two additional rulings from the same passage: marriage to a prohibited relative (mahram) and the religion of a child when one parent converts before birth. The general principle of ghayr mukhatabin applies to the past validity, but the mahram case has a unique exception: the marriage is annulled after conversion because the relationship itself is incompatible with Islamic marriage. The child's religion is decided by the better parent: if one is Muslim, the child is Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mahram scenario
The text describes a disbeliever marrying a mahram (e.g., sister) which was permissible in Magian religion. After conversion, the ruling is that they are separated (فرق بينهما).
Note that the marriage is not invalid because it was invalid in disbelief; it was valid there. The annulment is due to the ongoing prohibition after conversion.
2
Understand the reasoning for annulment
The reason is 'al-mahramiyya tunafi baqa' al-nikah' (the relationship of mahram conflicts with the continuation of the marriage). Unlike lack of witnesses or iddah, which do not affect continuity, mahram is a permanent bar.
Witnesses are only required at inception (in‘iqad), not for continuation (baqa'). iddah expires. Mahram never expires.
3
Apply to child's religion
The passage states 'الطفل مسلم' implying that if one parent is Muslim at the time of birth, the child is Muslim. This follows the principle that the child follows the better religion.
This is a separate ruling not dependent on the marriage discussion.

Source Text

كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الرقيق والكافر فإن أسلم المتزوجان بلا شهود أو في عدة كافر معتقدين ذلك، أقرا عليه ، وإن أسلم الزوجان المحرّمان فُرِّق بينهما ، والطفل مسلم [٢]قوله : فإن أسلم ؛ شروع في مباحث أنكحة الكفّار، وحاصله : إن الكافر إذا تزوج بكافرة بدون سماع الشهود، أو تزوج امرأة كانت معتدة من كافر طلقها، أو مات عنها، وكان ذلك جائزاً عندهم، ثم أسلم الزوجان أبقيا على نكاحهما ؛ وذلك لأن كل نكاح حرام بين المسلمين لفقد شرط يجوز في حقهم إذا اعتقدوا جوازه ، ويقرون عليه بعد الإسلام ؛ لأنا أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون. والسر فيه : إنّهم لا يخاطبون حالة كفرهم بحقوق الإسلام، فإذا نكحوا بطريق جائز عندهم تم ذلك النكاح ، ويبقى ذلك بعد الإسلام أيضاً. وفي مسألة العدّة خلاف لأبي يوسف ومحمد ، هما يقولان : إن حرمة نكاح معتدة الغير مجمع على حرمته، وحرمة النكاح بلا شهود مختلف فيه عندنا، ونحن نقول : لا أثر لهذا الفرق ، فإن الكفّارَ غير مخاطبين حالة كفرهم لا بهذا ولا بهذا. [٣) قوله : وإن أسلم ؛ أي إن نكح كافر محرماً من المحارم، ثم أسلما يفرق بينهما لا لبطلان النكاح في الكفر، فإنه عند المجوس جائز ، بل لأن المحرمية تنافي بقاء النكاح بخلاف ما مر ، فإن الشهادة لا تشترط حالة البقاء، وكذا العدة لا تنافيه. [٤]قوله : والطفل ... الخ ؛ يعني إن كان أحد الأبوين مسلماً، والآخر كافراً بأن (۱) (الهداية (٦) : ٢٥٢).
Book of Marriage, Chapter on the Marriage of the Slave and the Disbeliever. If the two spouses who married without witnesses, or during a waiting period (iddah) of a disbeliever, believing that to be permissible, they are confirmed upon (conversion). And if the two spouses who are in a prohibited relationship (mahram) embrace Islam, they are separated. The child (is) Muslim. [2] His statement: 'if they embrace Islam' is an entry into the topics of the marriages of disbelievers. Its gist: that if a disbeliever marries a disbelieving woman without the hearing of witnesses, or marries a woman who was in iddah from a disbeliever who divorced her or died from her, and that was permissible according to them, then the two spouses embrace Islam, they are kept on their marriage. That is because every marriage that is forbidden among Muslims due to the lack of a condition is permissible in their case if they believe in its permissibility, and they are confirmed upon it after Islam, because we are commanded to leave them with what they practice. The reason: they are not addressed during their state of disbelief with the rulings of Islam. So if they marry in a way permissible in their religion, that marriage is complete and remains after Islam as well. On the issue of iddah, there is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: they say that the prohibition of marrying a woman in the iddah of another is a matter of consensus, while the prohibition of marriage without witnesses is disputed among us. But we say: this distinction has no effect, because the disbelievers are not addressed during their disbelief with either of these. [3] His statement: 'and if they embrace Islam' means that if a disbeliever marries a mahram (prohibited relative) and then both embrace Islam, they are separated, not because the marriage is invalid in disbelief, for it is permissible among the Magians, but because the relationship of mahram conflicts with the continuation of the marriage, unlike what preceded, for witnesses are not required for the continuation, and likewise iddah does not conflict with it. [4] His statement: 'and the child' means that if one of the parents is Muslim and the other is a disbeliever, then... (ref: al-Hidayah 6:252)
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on marriages of disbelievers addresses three scenarios: marriage without witnesses, marriage during iddah, and marriage to a mahram. The general rule is that marriages contracted in disbelief are upheld after conversion to Islam if they were considered valid by the couple's religion and they acted in good faith. The legal reasoning (سر) is that disbelievers are not addressed by Islamic rulings during their disbelief; therefore, their past marriage is judged by their own law. For marriage without witnesses: it is upheld because the lack of witnesses does not affect the continuation of the marriage. For marriage during iddah: there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that marriage to a woman in iddah is prohibited by consensus (even among earlier religions), so it should be invalid after Islam. But the author rejects this distinction: since disbelievers are not addressed by Islamic laws at the time, the consensus among Muslims is irrelevant to them. For marriage to a mahram: it is not automatically invalid in disbelief (Magians allow it), but after Islam, the couple must be separated because the prohibition arising from lineage persists and is incompatible with marriage continuation. The child's ruling: if one parent is Muslim at the time of birth, the child is Muslim. The passage relies on the principle that disbelievers are not addressed (غير مخاطبين) with subsidiary rulings (furu') but are addressed with fundamental beliefs (usul al-iman).

Key Terms
محرمان (muḥrimān): A man and woman who are prohibited from marrying each other due to blood relationship, affinity, or fosterage (mahram).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a marriage to a mahram annulled upon conversion, whereas a marriage without witnesses is confirmed?
A) Because mahram marriages are always invalid even in disbelief
B) Because the prohibition of mahram is permanent and conflicts with marriage continuation, while lack of witnesses affects only the initial contract
C) Because witnesses are required only after conversion
D) Because the text does not address mahram marriages

Key Takeaway
Marriage to a mahram is annulled after conversion due to permanent prohibition, while other defects like lack of witnesses or iddah do not affect continuation.

Validity of Marriages of Converts: The Principle of Non-Addressability
Ruling on marriage validity of converts to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of marriages of converts who married without witnesses or during 'iddah, and explain the rationale and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Disbelievers are not addressed by Islamic rulings before conversion—their prior valid marriage remains valid.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains a key Hanafi principle: the marriages of disbelievers, even if defective by Islamic standards, are upheld upon conversion if they were valid in their own religion. The text also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) over the specific case of marriage during 'iddah. Understanding this principle prevents unnecessary separation of converts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two cases: marriage without witnesses, and marriage during 'iddah. Both are defective in Islamic law but may be valid in the couple's prior religion.
2
Recognize the general rule
If a disbelieving couple marries in a way permissible in their religion, then both convert to Islam, they are left as they are—their marriage is confirmed and not re-evaluated.
This is because we are commanded to leave them to their own religious practices.
3
Understand the rationale
The underlying principle is that disbelievers are not addressed (mukhatab) with Islamic rulings (khitab) before their conversion. Therefore, the marriage is judged by their own law, not by Islamic standards.
This principle applies to all such marriages, not just the two examples.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad object that the prohibition of marrying a woman in 'iddah is universally agreed upon (mujma' 'alayhi) and thus should invalidate the marriage even for converts. Abu Hanifa responds that the distinction is irrelevant because both 'iddah and witness requirements are not addressed to disbelievers.
The text side with Abu Hanifa, and this is the mufta bihi position.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Any marriage that was valid in the couple's prior religion remains valid after conversion, even if it lacks conditions or contains prohibitions under Islamic law.
Exception: marriages that are invalid even in the prior religion (e.g., incest) would not be upheld.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If the two spouses who married without witnesses or during an 'iddah period of a disbeliever, both believing it to be permissible, then they both become Muslim, they are left as they are. He said: 'His saying: "If they become Muslim" begins the discussion of the marriages of disbelievers. Its summary: If a disbeliever marries a disbelieving woman without hearing witnesses, or marries a woman who was in 'iddah from a disbeliever who divorced her or died, and that was permissible according to them, then both become Muslim, their marriage is confirmed. This is because every marriage that is haram among Muslims due to lack of a condition is permissible in their regard if they believe it permissible, and they are confirmed in it after Islam because we have been commanded to leave them and what they deem lawful. Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of marriage of disbelievers who convert to Islam. The key principle is that disbelievers are not bound by Islamic legal rulings before their conversion. Therefore, if they married according to their own religious laws—even if that marriage would be invalid under Islamic law due to lack of witnesses or because the woman was in her 'iddah period—that marriage is considered valid after they accept Islam, provided they believed it was lawful at the time. The commentator explains the underlying rationale: we are commanded to leave them to their own religious practices. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad object that the prohibition of marrying a woman in 'iddah is universally agreed upon. The response is that since they were not Muslims at the time, they were not addressed by Islamic rulings, so the distinction between 'iddah and lack of witnesses is irrelevant.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
شهود (shuhud): Witnesses required for a valid marriage contract in Islam.

Separation of Mahram Spouses Upon Conversion
Ruling on marriage of close relatives who convert to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a marriage between mahram relatives is annulled upon their conversion to Islam, distinguishing it from annulments based on missing conditions like witnesses or 'iddah.

Core Idea
Mahram marriage is invalid upon conversion because the prohibited relationship contradicts the essence of Islamic marriage.

When non-Muslims who are mahram to each other (e.g., brother and sister) convert to Islam, their prior marriage does not automatically become valid. The Hanafi school rules that they must be separated immediately. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this ruling, which hinges on the permanence of the mahram prohibition, and contrasts it with other situations where a marriage might continue despite defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
A non-Muslim man marries a mahram relative (e.g., his sister) in a religion that permits it (like Zoroastrianism). Then both convert to Islam.
The marriage was valid in their prior religion but not in Islam.
2
State the Ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that they must be separated immediately (furriqa baynahuma). The marriage becomes invalid (batil) upon conversion.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Understand the Reasoning
The author explains that the annulment is not because the marriage was invalid in the state of disbelief (it was valid according to their religion), but because the mahram relationship fundamentally contradicts the continuation of marriage in Islam. Mahramiyya (prohibited kinship) is a permanent bar that cannot be waived.
The key phrase: 'بل لأن المحرمية تنافي بقاء النكاح'
4
Contrast with Prior Cases
The text contrasts this with prior cases where witnesses were lacking or 'iddah was present. Those are procedural or temporary barriers, not intrinsic prohibitions. Mahramiyya, however, goes to the very essence of permissible marriage.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the different outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أسلَمَ الزوجان المحرّمان فُرِّق بينهما. قوله : وإن أسلم ; أي إن نكح كافر محرماً من المحارم، ثم أسلما يفرق بينهما لا لبطلان النكاح في الكفر، فإنه عند المجوس جائز ، بل لأن المحرمية تنافي بقاء النكاح بخلاف ما مر ، فإن الشهادة لا تشترط حالة البقاء، وكذا العدة لا تنافيه.
And if the two spouses who are mahram (close blood relatives) become Muslim, they are separated. He said: 'His saying: "And if they become Muslim" means: If a disbeliever marries a mahram relative, then they both become Muslim, they are separated not because the marriage was invalid in disbelief—since among the Magians it is permissible—but because the mahram relationship contradicts the continuation of marriage, unlike the previous case, because witnesses are not required for continuity, nor does 'iddah contradict it.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a disbeliever marries a mahram relative (such as a sister or daughter) according to their religion (like Magians who permit such marriages). Upon both converting to Islam, the marriage must be dissolved immediately. The commentator clarifies that this is not because the marriage was invalid in their state of disbelief (it was valid according to their law), but because the mahram relationship is fundamentally incompatible with the permanence of marriage in Islam. This contrasts with the previous case of lacking witnesses or 'iddah, which do not inherently contradict the continuation of marriage.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A close blood relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited in Islam, such as mother, sister, daughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They remain married because it was valid in their prior religion.
B) They are separated because the marriage is invalid from the start.
C) They are separated because mahram marriage is incompatible with Islam.
D) They remain married if they had witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A mahram marriage is not salvageable upon conversion because the prohibition is permanent and intrinsic to Islamic marriage.

Child's Religion When Parents Differ in Faith
Ruling on child's religion in mixed-faith marriages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the religion of a child born to parents of different faiths, apply the principle that the child follows the better religion, and identify the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A child follows the better religion of its parents; Islam is superior, so if one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim.

Determining the religion of a child when parents belong to different faiths is a practical issue in Islamic law. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling based on the principle of following the better religion, supported by the hadith 'Islam is exalted and nothing is exalted above it.' This lesson explains the ruling and its application to various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
The child is Muslim if one parent is Muslim (by birth or conversion). If parents are a Magian and a Kitabi, the child is Kitabi because Kitabi is better.
The principle is 'the child follows the better of the two parents in religion.'
2
Apply the principle to different cases
Consider cases: Muslim + non-Muslim (child Muslim), Muslim + Kitabi (child Muslim), Kitabi + Magian (child Kitabi). The ruling is consistent.
The hadith 'Islam is exalted' provides the basis for Islam's superiority.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Islam is exalted and nothing is exalted above it' (الإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى) is the proof. It indicates that Islam takes precedence, so the child is attributed to Islam when one parent is Muslim.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Ya'la and others; it is accepted by Hanafis.
4
Understand the legal consequence
The child is considered Muslim legally: given Muslim name, Islamic funeral rites, inheritance as Muslim, etc. This applies even if the non-Muslim parent is the primary caregiver.
The ruling is a legal attribution (hukm), not necessarily reflecting the child's personal faith until maturity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطفل مسلم إن كان أحد أبويه مسلماً، أو أسلم أحدهما، وكتابي إن كان بين مجوسي وكتابي; لأنَّ الطفل يتبع خير الأبوين دينا. قوله : والطفل ... الخ ; يعني إن كان أحد الأبوين مسلماً، والآخر كافراً بأن إن كان أحد أبويه مسلماً ، أو أسلم أحدهما ، وكتابي إن كان بين مجوسي وكتابي ; لأنَّ الطفل يتبع خير الأبوين دينا. وفي إسلام زوج المجوسية، أو امرأة الكافر) : أي سواء كان كتابياً ، أو مجوسياً تزوّج المسلم كتابية أو أسلم أحدهما ، فالمولود بينهما مسلم حكماً حتى لو مات يُجهز كتجهيز المسلمين، وأصله حديث : «الإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى، أخرجه أبو يعلى، وغيره.
And the child is Muslim if one of its parents is Muslim, or if one of them becomes Muslim, and a Kitabi (People of the Book) if between a Magian and a Kitabi; because the child follows the better of the parents in religion. His saying: 'The child...' means: If one of the parents is Muslim and the other is a disbeliever, either by one being Muslim or by one converting, and a Kitabi if between a Magian and a Kitabi; because the child follows the better of the parents in religion. And regarding the Islam of a Magian husband or the wife of a disbeliever: whether the husband is a Kitabi or Magian, if a Muslim marries a Kitabi or one of them converts, the child between them is considered Muslim legally, so that if he dies he is prepared as Muslims are prepared. The basis of this is the hadith: 'Islam is exalted and nothing is exalted above it', narrated by Abu Ya'la and others.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage determines the religion of a child born to parents of different faiths. The key principle is that the child follows the better religion of the two parents. If one parent is Muslim (whether by birth or conversion), the child is Muslim. If one parent is a Kitabi (Jewish or Christian) and the other is a Magian (Zoroastrian), the child is considered a Kitabi because Kitabi is better than Magian. The hadith 'Islam is exalted and nothing is exalted above it' supports the ruling that Islam takes precedence. This also applies to the case where a Muslim woman is married to a Magian or a Muslim man marries a Kitabi woman; the child is Muslim. The practical consequence: the child is treated as Muslim in all legal matters, including funeral rites.

Key Terms
كتابي (Kitabi): A follower of a revealed scripture, i.e., Jew or Christian.
مجوسي (Majusi): A Zoroastrian (Magian).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The child is Christian because the mother is Christian.
B) The child is Muslim because the father is Muslim.
C) The child chooses his religion at puberty.
D) The child is considered Kitabi.

Key Takeaway
If one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim by legal ruling, following the principle that Islam is exalted and the child takes the better religion.

6 min
The Child Follows the Better Religion of the Parents
Ruling on child following the better religion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the religion of a child when one parent is Muslim, and identify the exception for the child of zina.

Core Idea
If one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim — Islam prevails over all.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi family law: the religion of a child is determined by the 'better' of its two parents. Islam is considered the highest religion, so if one parent converts or is already Muslim, the child is legally Muslim. The proof is a hadith about the supremacy of Islam. We will also explore an intra-madhhab dispute about children born from zina, where the father is not legally recognized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Read the first line: 'the child follows the better of the parents in religion' (yatba'u khayr al-abawayn dīnan). This is the foundation of the ruling.
2
Apply the Principle to Scenarios
If one parent is Muslim (by birth or conversion), the child is Muslim. If one is Kitabi and the other Magian, the child is Kitabi.
Kitabi = Scriptuary (Jew/Christian); Magian = Zoroastrian.
3
Locate the Dalil
The hadith 'Islam is high and nothing is higher than it' (الإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى) proves the superiority of Islam, so it prevails.
Even though this hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), it is relied upon here.
4
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The apparent wording excludes the child of zina (walad al-zina), but some later Hanafis (e.g., Radd al-Muhtar) rule the child is Muslim based on the fitra hadith.
The mufta bihi in later fatwa is that the child of zina is Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٣] قوله أو امرأة الكافر؛ سواء كانت وثنيةً أو مجوسية أو كتابية، وحاصل صور إسلام أ م أحد الزوجين على اثنين وثلاثين ؛ لأنهما إما أن يكونا كتابيين أو مجوسيين ، الزوج كتابي، وهي مجوسية، أو بالعكس. (۱) في مسند الروياني (۲) : ٤٠٢) ، ومعرفة السنن (۱۱ : ۳۹۱) ، و(سنن الدارقطني (۳ : ٢٥٢)، و«شرح معاني الآثار»(٣: ٢٥٧)، وغيرها. (۲) رد المحتار» (۳ : ۱۹۷)، وقال : ويظهر لي الحكم بالإسلام للحديث الصحيح كل مولود يولد على الفطرة حتى يكون أبواه هما اللذان يهودانه أو ينصرانه»، فإنهم قالوا : إنه جعل اتفاقهما ناقلاً له عن الفطرة، فإذا لم يتفقا بقي على أصل الفطرة أو على ما هو أقرب إليها، حتى لو كان أحدهما مجوسياً والآخر كتابياً فهو كتابي كما يأتي. وهنا ليس له أبوان متفقان فيبقى على الفطرة، ولأنهم قالوا إن إلحاقه بالمسلم أو بالكتابي أنفع له ، ولا شك أن النظر لحقيقة الجزئية أنفع له، وأيضاً حيث نظروا للجزئية في تلك المسائل احتياطاً فلينظر إليها هنا احتياطاً أيضاً، فإن الاحتياط بالدين أولى ؛ ولأن الكفر أقبح القبيح فلا ينبغي الحكم به على شخص بدون أمر صريح ؛ ولأنهم قالوا في حرمة بنته من الزنا : إن الشرع قطع النسبة إلى الزاني لما فيها من إشاعة الفاحشة فلم يثبت النفقة والإرث لذلك، وهذا لا ينفي النسبة الحقيقية ؛ لأن الحقائق لا مرد لها فمن ادعى أنه لا بد من النسبة الشرعية فعليه البيان.
If one of his parents is Muslim, or one of them accepts Islam, and (the child is) a kitabi if between a Magian and a Kitabi, because the child follows the better of the parents in religion. (And regarding the Islam of the husband of a Magian woman, or the wife of a disbeliever): meaning whether he was a Kitabi or a Magian, a Muslim married a Kitabi or one of them accepted Islam, so the child between them is a Muslim by ruling, even if he dies he is prepared like the preparations of Muslims. Its origin is the hadith: 'Islam is high and nothing is higher than it,' reported by Abu Ya'la and others. [1] His saying: one of his parents; its apparent meaning excludes the child of zina, so if a Muslim commits zina with a Christian woman and she gives birth to a child, he is not a Muslim, and some gave a fatwa of his Islam, and it was preferred in Radd al-Muhtar. (2) His saying: the Magian woman; perhaps he meant other than a Kitabi, for if the husband of a Kitabi accepts Islam, there is no display (of Islam) nor separation due to permissibility of a Muslim marrying a Kitabi, not his marriage to other disbelieving women. Or it is said: when the ruling of the Magian woman is known, the ruling of others is known by primary analogy. [3] His saying: or the wife of a disbeliever; whether she is an idolater, Magian, or Kitabi. The total forms of Islam of one of the two spouses are thirty-two: because they are either both Kitabi, both Magian, the husband Kitabi and she Magian, or vice versa. (1) In Musnad al-Ruyani (2:402), Ma'rifat al-Sunan (11:391), Sunan al-Daraqutni (3:252), Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar (3:257), etc. (2) Radd al-Muhtar (3:197), and he said: 'It appears to me the ruling of Islam due to the authentic hadith: Every child is born on the fitra until his parents make him Jewish or Christian.' For they said: He made their agreement a transfer from the fitra, so if they do not agree, he remains on the original fitra or on what is closer to it, so that if one is Magian and the other Kitabi, he is Kitabi as will come. And here he does not have two agreeing parents, so he remains on the fitra. Also because they said that attaching him to the Muslim or the Kitabi is more beneficial to him, and there is no doubt that considering the reality of the partiality is more beneficial to him. Also, since they considered partiality in those matters as a precaution, let it be considered here as a precaution as well, for precaution in religion is more important. Also because disbelief is the ugliest of ugly things, so it is not proper to judge it on a person without clear order. Also because they said regarding the prohibition of his daughter from zina: The Law severed the lineage from the fornicator due to what it contains of spreading lewdness, so maintenance and inheritance are not established for that reason. This does not negate the real lineage because realities cannot be repelled. So whoever claims that legal lineage is necessary must provide proof.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah deals with the legal ruling on the religion of a child when one parent converts to Islam. The author first states a general rule: a child follows the better religion of its parents (yatba'u khayr al-abawayn dīnan). Thus, if one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim; if one is a Kitabi (Scriptuary) and the other Magian, the child is considered Kitabi. The proof is the hadith 'Islam is high and nothing is higher than it' (al-Islāmu ya'lū wa lā yu'lā), indicating the superiority of Islam. A longer discussion follows on the case of a child born from zina (fornication) between a Muslim man and a Christian woman. The apparent wording of 'one of his parents' excludes such a child because the fornicator is not legally a father. However, some scholars (including the author of Radd al-Muhtar) opine that the child should be considered Muslim, relying on the hadith that every child is born on fitra (natural disposition), and since the parents are not in agreement (one Muslim, one non-Muslim), the child remains on fitra, which is Islam. The passage also clarifies that a Magian is used as an example for a non-Scriptuary; the ruling applies by primary analogy to other disbelievers. The author then lists the possible scenarios when one spouse becomes Muslim, noting thirty-two forms based on the spouses' original religions and whether they are in dar al-Islam or dar al-harb. Importantly, a change within non-Muslim religions (e.g., Christian converting to Judaism) is ignored because all disbelief is one milla. The key point is that the child's Islam is established by the mere fact of one parent being Muslim, without need for a judicial decision.

Key Terms
خير الأبوين ديناً (khayr al-abawayn dīnan): The better of the two parents in religion; legally, the parent who follows the superior religion (Islam over other religions, Kitabi over Magian).
ولد الزنا (walad al-zinā): A child born from fornication (zina), for whom legal lineage (nasab) is not established to the father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a non-Muslim couple has a child, and then the father converts to Islam, what is the religion of the child according to the Hanafi school?
A) The child remains non-Muslim because both parents were originally non-Muslim.
B) The child becomes Muslim because the father is now Muslim and the child follows the better religion.
C) The child is considered Kitabi if the mother is Kitabi, regardless of the father's conversion.
D) The child's religion is determined only when the child reaches puberty.

Key Takeaway
A child's religion is determined at birth by the better religion of its parents; if one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim.

The Procedure When One Spouse Converts to Islam: Presenting Islam and Judicial Separation
Procedure when one spouse converts and Islam is offered to the other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure when one spouse converts to Islam and explain why presenting Islam to the other spouse is mandatory before judicial separation.

Core Idea
Islam is not a cause of separation – refusal after presentation triggers the judge's act.

When one spouse embraces Islam, the marriage does not automatically dissolve. The Hanafi school requires that the non-Muslim spouse be formally presented with Islam (ʿarḍ al-Islām). This lesson examines the ruling, its conditions, and the evidence from the Quran and Sunnah. You will learn why this step is essential before any separation can take place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that upon one spouse's conversion, the immediate legal requirement is to present Islam to the other spouse. This is a wajib step.
It is not optional; it is a necessary procedure.
2
Examine the conditions for the presentation
The other spouse must be sane, adult, or a child who understands religions. If the child is insane, the offer is made to the parents. Understand why these conditions matter.
The condition ensures the recipient can meaningfully respond.
3
Trace the evidence (dalīl)
The primary evidence is the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ not immediately separating the daughter of al-Walid and Safwan. Also, Quran 60:10 prohibits Muslims from remaining married to disbelievers, but the separation is attributed to refusal, not to Islam itself.
The hadith is from Muwatta Malik (zannī al-thubūt).
4
Contrast with the Shāfiʿī position
Some scholars hold that conversion immediately dissolves the marriage. The Hanafī response is that the Prophet ﷺ delayed, showing that an offer must be made.
This is a legitimate ikhtilāf (disagreement).
5
State the muftā bihī position
The relied-upon Hanafī ruling: the judge must present Islam; if the spouse refuses, the judge pronounces separation (tafrīq).
Until the judge acts, the marriage remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعرضُ الإسلام على الآخر، فإن أسلم فهي له، وإلا فرق. [١] قوله : على الآخر: أي إذا كان عاقلاً بالغاً أو صبياً يعقل الأديان ؛ لأن ردته معتبرة، فكذا آباؤه، وإن كان الصبي مجنوناً عُرضَ على أبويه، فإن أسلم أحدهما بقي النكاح ؛ لأنه يتبع حكماً خير الأبوين ديناً. والوجه في العرض : أنه لا بُدَّ من سبب تضاف إليه الفرقة ؛ لعدم حل النكاح بين الكفّار وبين المسلمين لا ابتداء ولا بقاء ؛ لقوله : فَإِنْ عَلِمْتُمُوهُنَّ مُؤْمِنَاتِ فَلَا تَرْجِعُوهُنَّ إِلَى الكفَّارِ لا هُنَّ مِلٌ لَمْ وَلَا هُمْ يَحلُونَ منَ (٢) ، وقوله : وَلَا تُمْسِكُوا بِعِصَم الكوافي ) (۳) ، والإسلام صفة عاصمة لا ينبغي أن تضاف الفرقةُ إليه ، فلا بُدَّ من عرض الإسلام على الآخر حتى تضاف إلى آبائه، ولو أسلم فلا تفريق. ويشهد له ما في «الموطأ»: «أن ابنة الوليد كانت تحت صفوان بن أبي أمية فأسلمت يوم فتح مكة وهرب زوجها ، فلم يفرق رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بينهما حتى أسلم صفوان واستقرت عنده)، وذكر الطحاوي: «أن عمر له فَرَّق بين نصراني ونصرانية بإبائه عن الإسلام». كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره. [٢] قوله : وإلا ؛ أي وإن لم يسلم الآخر بل أبي عنه. (۳) قوله : فرق ؛ بصيغة المجهول من التفريق : أي فرَّقَ القاضي وما لم يفرق، فهي على حالة.
Islam is presented to the other (spouse). If he accepts Islam, then she is his (wife); otherwise they are separated. [1] His saying: to the other; meaning if he is sane and adult, or a child who understands religions, because his apostasy is considered, so likewise his parents. If the child is insane, it is presented to his parents. If one of them accepts Islam, the marriage remains because he follows by ruling the better of the parents in religion. The wisdom in the presentation is that there must be a cause to which the separation is attributed, because the marriage between disbelievers and Muslims is not permissible initially nor by continuation, due to His saying: 'Then if you know them to be believers, do not return them to the disbelievers; they are not lawful for them, nor are they lawful for them' (Quran 60:10), and His saying: 'And do not hold on to the marriage ties of disbelieving women' (Quran 60:10). And Islam is a protecting quality; it is not proper to attribute separation to it, so it is necessary to present Islam to the other so that it (separation) is attributed to his refusal. If he accepts Islam, there is no separation. Evidence for this is in al-Muwatta: 'The daughter of al-Walid was under Safwan ibn Umayya; she embraced Islam on the day of the conquest of Mecca, and her husband fled. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not separate them until Safwan embraced Islam and settled with her.' And al-Tahawi mentioned that 'Umar separated between a Christian man and woman due to his refusal of Islam.' This is in al-Fath and others. [2] His saying: otherwise; i.e., if the other does not accept Islam but refuses it. [3] His saying: they are separated; in the passive voice from tafriq: meaning the judge separates them, and as long as he has not separated, they are upon their (marriage) state.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage details the Hanafi procedure when one spouse embraces Islam. The ruling is that the other spouse is presented with Islam (yu'radu al-Islam). If he or she accepts, the marriage continues; if not, the judge separates them (farraqa). The commentary clarifies that the presentation is required only for a spouse who is sane, adult, or a discerning child. If the child is insane, the offer is made to his parents; acceptance by one parent preserves the marriage because the child follows the better religion. The reason for the presentation is that separation cannot be directly attributed to Islam itself, as Islam is a noble and protecting quality. Instead, the separation must be linked to the spouse's refusal to accept Islam. The proof includes Quranic verses (Surah al-Mumtahinah 60:10) forbidding Muslim women from remaining with non-Muslim husbands and prohibiting Muslims from holding onto marriage ties with disbelieving wives. A hadith from al-Muwatta relates that the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not immediately separate a couple when the wife converted; he waited until the husband converted. This shows that the marriage is not automatically dissolved upon conversion; an offer must be made. The passage also notes a practice of Umar al-Faruq who separated a Christian couple when the husband refused Islam. The key point is that the judge's ruling of separation is necessary; until then, the marriage remains valid.

Key Terms
عرض الإسلام (ʿarḍ al-Islām): Presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse after the other's conversion, giving them an opportunity to accept.
فرق (farraqa): The judge's act of separating the spouses by divorce (tafriq) due to refusal of Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens when a Christian husband converts to Islam while his wife remains Christian?
A) The marriage is automatically dissolved immediately upon his conversion.
B) The wife is offered Islam; if she accepts, the marriage continues; if she refuses, the judge separates them.
C) The marriage continues as long as the wife remains Christian; no action is required.
D) The wife must be divorced by the husband immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that Islam be presented to the non-converting spouse before any separation occurs, based on the practice of the Prophet and the principle that separation is attributed to refusal, not to Islam itself.

Presentation of Islam to Non-Muslim Spouse Upon Conversion
Explains the ruling and conditions when a spouse converts to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse upon the other's conversion, explain the proof from Quran and Sunnah, and identify the conditions for the presentation.

Core Idea
Conversion does not dissolve the marriage until Islam is presented and the spouse refuses.

When one spouse in a non-Muslim marriage converts to Islam, the marriage does not automatically dissolve. Instead, the judge must present Islam to the other spouse, giving them the opportunity to accept and preserve the union. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the conditions that govern the process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse after one spouse converts. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) for the judge based on the need to avoid an invalid marriage.
The marriage is not permissible between a Muslim and a non-Muslim at any time.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary proof: Quranic verse 60:10 (Surah al-Mumtahina) which prohibits returning believing women to disbelieving husbands, plus the hadith from al-Muwatta about Safwan and his wife.
The verse implies separation, but the hadith shows the Prophet waited, indicating presentation is needed.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse states that believing women are not lawful for disbelieving men, so the marriage must end. But the separation is attributed to the spouse's refusal, not to the conversion itself, as shown by the Prophet's delay.
The rationale: the judge cannot attribute separation to the noble quality of Islam; a cause (refusal) must be present.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that the marriage dissolves automatically upon conversion without presentation. The Hanafi response is that the hadith and practice of the Prophet support presentation.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is the stronger one due to the direct precedent of the Prophet's action and the rulings of Umar. It is the mufta bihi position.
The verse is general, and the hadith specifies the procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعرضُ الإسلام على الآخرِ، فإن أسلم فهي له، وإلا فرق. وعلى كل فالمسلم الزوج أو الزوجة، وفي كل من الثمانية، إما أن يكونا في دارنا، أو في دار الحرب، أو الزوج فقط في دارنا أو بالعكس، والمراد بالمجوسي في هذا المقام من ليس له كتاب سماوي، فيشمل الوثني وغيره. وقيد بالإسلام؛ لأن النصرانية إذا تهوّدت أو عكسه لا يلتفت إليهم؛ لأن الكفر كله ملة واحدة. كذا في «البحر». [١] قوله: على الآخر: أي إذا كان عاقلاً بالغاً أو صبياً يعقل الأديان؛ لأن ردته معتبرة، فكذا آباؤه، وإن كان الصبي مجنوناً عُرضَ على أبويه، فإن أسلم أحدهما بقي النكاح؛ لأنه يتبع حكماً خير الأبوين ديناً. والوجه في العرض: أنه لا بُدَّ من سبب تضاف إليه الفرقة؛ لعدم حل النكاح بين الكفار وبين المسلمين لا ابتداء ولا بقاء؛ لقوله تعالى: {فإن علمتموهن مؤمنات فلا ترجعوهن إلى الكفار لا هن حل لهم ولا هم يحلون لهن}، وقوله: {ولا تمسكوا بعصم الكوافر}، والإسلام صفة عاصمة لا ينبغي أن تضاف الفرقة إليه، فلا بد من عرض الإسلام على الآخر حتى تضاف إلى آبائه، ولو أسلم فلا تفريق. ويشهد له ما في «الموطأ»: أن ابنة الوليد كانت تحت صفوان بن أبي أمية فأسلمت يوم فتح مكة وهرب زوجها، فلم يفرق رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بينهما حتى أسلم صفوان واستقرت عنده، وذكر الطحاوي: أن عمر رضي الله عنه فرق بين نصراني ونصرانية بإبائه عن الإسلام. كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره.
Islam is presented to the other [spouse]; if he accepts Islam, she is his, otherwise they are separated. And in every case, the Muslim is the husband or the wife, and in all eight [cases], either both are in our abode, or in the abode of war, or only the husband is in our abode, or vice versa. And the meaning of 'Majusi' in this context is one who has no heavenly scripture, so it includes idol worshipers and others. And it is restricted to Islam because if a Christian becomes a Jew or vice versa, no attention is paid to them, because all disbelief is one religion. Thus in al-Bahr. [1] His saying: 'to the other': meaning if he is a sane adult, or a minor who understands religions, because his apostasy is considered, similarly his parents; if the minor is insane, it is presented to his parents; if one of them accepts Islam, the marriage remains, because it follows the ruling of the better of the two parents in religion. And the rationale for presentation: there must be a cause to which the separation is attributed, since marriage between disbelievers and Muslims is not permissible initially nor for continuance, due to His saying: {If you know them to be believers, do not return them to the disbelievers; they are not lawful for them, nor are they lawful for them} and His saying: {And do not hold on to the marriage ties with disbelieving women}, and Islam is a protective quality, so the separation should not be attributed to it; therefore, Islam must be presented to the other so that it is attributed to his parents, and if he accepts Islam, there is no separation. Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on what happens when one spouse in a non-Muslim marriage converts to Islam. The author explains that the other spouse is presented with Islam; if they accept, the marriage continues; if they refuse, the judge separates them. The commentary elaborates conditions: the spouse presented must be adult and sane, or if a minor, one who understands religions. If the minor is insane, the presentation is made to his parents. The proof is from the Qur'an (Surah al-Mumtahina, verses 10) which prohibits returning believing women to disbelieving husbands and commands Muslims not to hold on to marriage with disbelieving women. Additionally, a hadith from al-Muwatta shows the Prophet ﷺ did not immediately separate Safwan and his wife when she converted, but waited until Safwan also converted. This demonstrates that the separation is not automatic; it follows the presentation of Islam. The passage also notes that all disbelief is considered one religion, so a change from Christianity to Judaism is irrelevant; only conversion to Islam matters.

Key Terms
عرض الإسلام (ard al-Islam): The act of presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse after the other spouse has converted, giving them the opportunity to accept and preserve the marriage.
إباء (iba'): Refusal to accept Islam after being presented with it, leading to judicial separation.
فرقة (furqa): The judicial separation between spouses due to difference in religion after one converts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the conversion of one spouse does not automatically dissolve the marriage; the judge must present Islam to the other spouse; only upon refusal is the marriage terminated.

Nature of Separation: Talaq Ba'in vs Faskh
Discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute on talaq vs faskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the nature of separation upon refusal of Islam, and state the thamarah al-khilaf for the number of remaining divorces.

Core Idea
When a husband refuses Islam, the judge's separation counts as a ba'in divorce, not a faskh — the imamayn's view, not Abu Yusuf's.

The ruling that a husband's refusal leads to a judicial separation is clear, but what kind of separation is it? Abu Yusuf argued it was a simple annulment (faskh), while the school's core position holds it is an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in). This dispute affects how many divorces the husband has left if the couple later reconciles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ikhtilaf
The text states that Abu Yusuf considers the separation a faskh, while the school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) consider it a talaq ba'in. Note that the wife's refusal does not produce a divorce.
2
Analyze each side's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that refusal is shared by both spouses, so it cannot be a divorce from the husband alone. The imamayn counter that the husband's refusal prevents him from keeping her with kindness, so the judge substitutes for the husband in releasing her (tasrih bi-ihsan).
The woman is not qualified to give divorce, so the judge acts only for the husband.
3
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
If the husband later converts and remarries her, according to the imamayn he has two divorces left (one already counted); according to Abu Yusuf he has three (no divorce counted). Thus, a later double divorce from the imamayn's view yields baynunah mughallazah.
This practical difference is the fruit (thamarah) of the dispute.
4
Apply to special cases
If the husband is insane, Islam is presented to his parents; if they refuse, the judge separates him and it counts as a divorce. For a non-discerning minor, the presentation is delayed until he becomes discerning.
The judge can issue a divorce on behalf of those who cannot divorce themselves due to necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو طلاق بائن لو أبي، لا لو أبت. (وهو): أي التفريق، (طلاق بائن لو أبي، لا لو أبت) زوجته حتى لو مات الزوج قبل أن تسلّم امرأته وجب لها المهر بكماله، وإن لم يدخل بها؛ لأن النكاح كان قائماً وتقرر بالموت. [١] قوله : طلاق ... الخ؛ اختلف فيه، فقال أبو يوسف له : الفرقة ليست بطلاق، بل فسخ، وعندها : طلاق بائن. وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيما لو أسلم الزوج بعد التفريق وتزوجها يملك عليها تطليقتين عندها، وثلاث تطليقات عنده، فتبين منه بينونة مغلظة عندها باثنتين لسبقه إحدى الطلقات، وعنده بثلاث، وفائدة تقييد الطلاق عندها بالبائن أنه لو أسلم الزوج بعد الإباء لا يملك الرجعة في أيام العدة. له : إن الإباء يشترك فيه الزوجان، فلا يمكن جعل التفريق الحاصل به طلاقاً؛ لعدم إمكان الطلاق من جانب المرأة، وهما يقولان : بالإباء امتنع الإمساك بالمعروف مع قدرته عليه، فينوب القاضي منابه في التسريح بإحسان، أما المرأة فليست بأهل لأن تطلق، فلا ينوب القاضي منابها عند إبائها. كذا في «الهداية» وشروحها. (۲) قوله : لو أبى؛ أي الزوج، وكذا من في حكمه في اعتبار الإباء، فإن الزوج إذا كان مجنوناً يُعْرَضُ الإسلام على أبويه فإن أسلما أو أسلم أحدهما، فهي له، وإن أبى كل منهما فَرَّقَ القاضي، وكان ذلك طلاقاً، وأما إذا كان الزوج صبياً غير مميز، فينتظر للعرض كونه مميزاً. فإن قلت : كيف يصح هذا مع ما تقرر في موضعه : إنّ الصبي والمجنون ليسا بأهل للطلاق، وإن كان الصبي مميزا. قلت : المراد به أنه لا يقعُ منهما طلاق ابتداءً؛ لكونه ضرراً محضاً، وأما عند الحاجة فهو مشروع عنهما بإنابة القاضي منابهما. كذا في كشف الأسرار شرح أصول البزدوي، وغيره.
And it is a ba'in divorce if the husband refuses, not if the wife refuses. (And it): i.e., the separation, (is a ba'in divorce if the husband refuses, not if the wife refuses) his wife so that if the husband dies before his wife converts, her entire mahr becomes obligatory, even if he did not consummate the marriage, because the marriage was valid and confirmed by death. [1] His saying: 'divorce...etc.'; there is disagreement about it. Abu Yusuf said: The separation is not a divorce, but a dissolution (faskh), while according to us it is a ba'in divorce. The fruit of the dispute appears if the husband converts after the separation and marries her: according to us he has two divorces left over her, while according to him he has three divorces, so she becomes separated from him with a severe separation (baynunah mughallazah) according to us with two (divorces) because one of the divorces preceded, and according to him with three. And the benefit of restricting it to a ba'in divorce according to us is that if the husband converts after his refusal, he has no right to take her back during the waiting period. His evidence: Refusal is shared by both spouses, so it is not possible to make the resulting separation a divorce, because divorce is not possible from the woman's side. And they (i.e., Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say: By refusal, he prevented holding on to marriage with kindness while being capable of it, so the judge substitutes for him in releasing with kindness. As for the woman, she is not qualified to give divorce, so the judge does not substitute for her when she refuses. Thus in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. (2) His saying: 'if he refuses': i.e., the husband, and likewise whoever is in his ruling regarding the consideration of refusal. If the husband is insane, Islam is presented to his parents; if they both accept or one of them accepts, she is his; if both refuse, the judge separates them, and that is a divorce. As for if the husband is a non-discerning minor, he is waited for until he becomes discerning. If you ask: How can this be valid when it is established elsewhere that the minor and the insane are not qualified to give divorce, even if the minor is discerning? I say: The meaning is that they do not initiate divorce because it is pure harm, but when necessary, it is legislated for them by the judge substituting for them. Thus in Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi, and others.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage focuses on the legal nature of the separation when a spouse refuses Islam. The author first states the ruling: if the husband refuses, the separation is a ba'in divorce; if the wife refuses, it is not a divorce (i.e., the separation is simply a dissolution). Then a major intra-Hanafi dispute is introduced: Abu Yusuf holds that the separation is not a divorce but a faskh (annulment), while the relied-upon position (imamayn) holds it is a ba'in divorce. The practical consequence (thamara) is significant: if the husband later converts and remarries her, according to the imamayn he has only two divorces left (since one already occurred), but according to Abu Yusuf he has three divorces (since the separation was a faskh, not a divorce, so no divorce counted). Thus, if he later divorces her twice (making three total according to imamayn), she becomes permanently prohibited (baynunah mughallazah). The passage also explains the reasoning: Abu Yusuf argues that refusal is mutual, so it cannot be attributed solely to the husband as a divorce; the imamayn argue that the husband's refusal constitutes a failure to keep her with kindness, so the judge acts on his behalf to release her with kindness (tasrih bi-ihsan). The text also addresses special cases: if the husband is insane, the presentation is to his parents; if they refuse, the judge separates with a divorce. For a non-discerning minor, the presentation is delayed until he reaches discernment. The author resolves an apparent contradiction: how can a minor or insane person effect a divorce? The answer is that they do not initiate divorce themselves; the judge acts on their behalf out of necessity.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately and does not allow the husband to take her back without a new marriage and consummation if it is a minor separation (ba'in baynunah sughra) or a major separation (baynunah mughallazah) if three divorces have been issued.
فسخ (faskh): An annulment of the marriage contract due to a legal impediment, not classified as a divorce.
إباء (iba'): Refusal to accept Islam after being presented with it, leading to judicial separation.
بينونة مغلظة (baynunah mughallazah): A severe separation requiring that the woman marry another man and divorce him before she can remarry the original husband; occurs after three divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of the judicial separation when the husband refuses to accept Islam?
A) It is a faskh (annulment) that does not count as a divorce.
B) It is a talaq ba'in (irrevocable divorce) that counts as one divorce.
C) It is a talaq raj'i (revocable divorce) allowing the husband to take her back during iddah.
D) It is a faskh that counts as one divorce.

Key Takeaway
When a husband refuses Islam, the judge's decree of separation is a talaq ba'in and counts as one divorce; this is the mufta bihi position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, not Abu Yusuf's faskh view.

The Nature of Tafriq Upon Iba': Talaq or Faskh?
Explains the nature of tafriq upon ibā' and mahr consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding tafriq upon ibā', state the mufta bihi position, and apply the mahr rules for each scenario.

Core Idea
If the husband refuses Islam, separation is talaq bā'in; if the wife refuses, it is faskh.

When one spouse converts to Islam and the other refuses, a judicial separation (tafriq) occurs. This lesson examines a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: is this separation a divorce (talaq) or an annulment (faskh)? The answer affects the number of divorces the husband retains and mahr obligations. We will explore the reasoning of Abu Yusuf versus Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the core ruling from the matn
The matn states: 'It is a final divorce if the husband refuses, not if the wife refuses.' Understand the baseline: husband's ibā' = talaq bā'in, wife's ibā' = faskh (annulment).
This is the majority opinion of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
2. Contrast Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf argues that both cases are faskh because refusal is a common ground and divorce cannot come from the wife. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: number of divorces remaining.
Abu Yusuf's position is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
3. Analyse the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish: the husband is capable of 'retention with kindness' but refuses, so the judge substitutes for him in releasing (talaq). The wife lacks that capacity, so the judge does faskh.
This is a talīl-based distinction.
4
4. Apply the mahr rules for each scenario
For husband's ibā': if before consummation, half mahr; after, full. For wife's ibā': only consummated wife gets full mahr; otherwise none, because the separation is from her side.
The mahr rule mirrors talaq vs faskh logic.
5
5. Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequences
The mufta bihi is the majority view. Upon remarriage after husband's ibā', he has only two divorces left, and cannot take her back during iddah.
This shapes fatwa practice.
