[2] His saying: and no mahr; meaning if the refusal is from the wife, and the judge separates them due to her refusal, then if she was not consummated, there is no mahr for her, because every separation from her side before mahr is confirmed by consummation cancels the mahr; and if she was consummated, she has the full mahr because the mahr is confirmed by consummation, and after it is confirmed it is not cancelled by anything. This is contrary to the husband's refusal, for the separation resulting from it is divorce, so its ruling is the ruling of divorce: if before consummation, half the specified mahr; otherwise the full, as previously mentioned.

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the core ruling from the matn: the separation (tafriq) resulting from a husband's refusal to embrace Islam is a final divorce (talaq bā'in), but if it is the wife who refuses, it is not a divorce—rather, it is an annulment (faskh). The commentator then expands on this, noting that if the husband dies before the wife surrenders, the full mahr is due because the marriage was valid at the time of death. Next, he introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the separation in both cases is annulment, not divorce, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the husband's refusal results in divorce. The practical consequence (thamara al-khilaf) is seen in the number of divorces the husband retains after remarrying: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, he has only two remaining divorces (since one has already occurred), while according to Abu Yusuf he has three. Also, the restriction 'bā'in' means that if the husband converts after refusing, he cannot take her back during her waiting period. The passage then provides the reasoning behind the positions: Abu Yusuf argues that since refusal can come from both spouses, it cannot be divorce because divorce cannot be initiated by the woman. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad counter that the husband is able to retain the wife with kindness but refuses, so the judge substitutes for him in releasing her with kindness; for the woman, she is not qualified to divorce, so the judge does not substitute for her. The text then addresses special cases: if the husband is insane, Islam is presented to his parents; if they refuse, the judge separates them and it counts as divorce. For a minor who is not discerning, one waits until he becomes discerning. The commentator anticipates an objection: how can a minor or insane person be considered for divorce? The answer is that initially, divorce from them is not valid due to harm, but when necessary, the judge may substitute for them. Finally, the passage discusses mahr: in the case of the wife's refusal, only the consummated wife gets full mahr; otherwise no mahr. This is because the separation is from her side before mahr is confirmed. In the husband's refusal, the separation is divorce, so the mahr follows the rules of divorce: half before consummation, full after.

Key Terms
إباء (ibā'): Refusal to embrace Islam when one spouse converts.
تفريق (tafrīq): Judicial separation ordered by the judge due to ibā'.
طلاق بائن (ṭalāq bā'in): A final divorce that does not allow revocability during the waiting period.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the marriage contract, not counted as a divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The classification of tafriq from ibā' as talaq or faskh depends on which spouse refuses; the mufta bihi distinguishes between them, affecting mahr and divorce counts.

Tafriq in Dar al-Harb: Waiting Period Substitutes for Refusal
Explaining separation in dar al-harb using condition substitution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on separation when one spouse converts in dar al-harb and explain why the waiting period serves as a substitute for the cause.

Core Idea
In dar al-harb, separation occurs after the waiting period, not immediately, due to absence of Islamic authority.

When a spouse converts to Islam in enemy territory (dar al-harb), the usual process of presenting Islam and judicial separation is impossible because there is no Islamic authority. The Hanafi school solves this by treating the passage of the waiting period (idda) as a condition that substitutes for the cause of separation. This lesson unpacks that legal reasoning and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The ruling applies when one spouse converts to Islam while both are in dar al-harb. The non-converting spouse remains upon their original religion (e.g., Magian or other unbeliever).
This is distinct from conversion in dar al-Islam, where the judge can immediately present Islam and separate upon refusal.
2
Understand the Obstacle
In dar al-harb, there is no Islamic judge or authority to carry out the presentation (ʿarḍ) of Islam to the non-converting spouse. Therefore, the usual cause of separation—explicit refusal after presentation—cannot be established.
The absence of jurisdiction (wilāyah) is the key obstacle.
3
Apply the Principle: Condition Substitutes for Cause
The Hanafi jurists invoke the usul principle that a condition (shart) can stand in for a cause (sabab) when the cause is impossible to implement. Here, the passing of the wife's waiting period (idda) is taken as the condition that triggers separation.
This is a form of istihsan or practical necessity.
4
Derive the Ruling
When the waiting period elapses and the non-converting spouse has not embraced Islam, the couple becomes automatically separated. The separation is enacted (wājib) after this period, not before.
The exact length of the waiting period depends on the wife's circumstances (e.g., three menstrual cycles for a free woman).
5
Note the Practical Difference from Dar al-Islam
In dar al-Islam, separation can occur immediately upon refusal after presentation; here, separation is delayed until the waiting period ends. This difference arises from the lack of authority in dar al-harb.
This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school; other schools may have different approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان ذلك في دارهم ) : أي إسلام زوج المجوسية ، أو امرأة الكافر (لم تبن ... الخ ؛ الوجه في ذلك على ما في «الهداية» وشروحها : إِنه لَمَّا أسلم أحدهما في دار الحرب مست الحاجة إلى التفريق ؛ إذ المشرك لا يصلح للمسلم ، ولا عَرْض هاهنا حتى يعرف به إباء الآخر عن الإسلام فيفـرق بـه لإنعدام يـد أهـل الإسلام عن أهل الحرب، وفقدان الولاية.
ومن المعلوم أن إقامة الشرط مقام العلّة عند تعذر إقامة العلة جائز، فأقمنا شرط
الفرقة، وهو مضي هذه المدة مقام السبب : أي الإباء فإذا مضت هذه المدة صار مضيها
And if that were in their abode: i.e., the Islam of the husband of a Magian woman, or the wife of an unbeliever, she does not separate... the reasoning for that, according to al-Hidayah and its commentaries, is: when one of them converts to Islam in dar al-harb, there is need for separation, because an idolater is not suitable for a Muslim, and there is no 'ard (presentation) here by which the refusal of the other to Islam would be known so that the judge separates them, due to the absence of the authority of the people of Islam over the people of war, and the absence of jurisdiction. And it is known that establishing the condition in place of the cause when it is impossible to establish the cause is permissible. So we have established the condition of separation, which is the passing of this period, in place of the cause, i.e., refusal. So when this period passes, its passing...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario when one spouse converts to Islam in enemy territory (dar al-harb), not in dar al-Islam. The commentator explains that the ruling of immediate separation does not apply here. The reason is that in dar al-harb, there is no Islamic authority to present Islam to the non-converting spouse or to enforce a judicial separation. Therefore, the Hanafi jurists have established a substitute: instead of the cause of separation (which is the explicit refusal to embrace Islam), we take the passing of a certain period (the waiting period of the wife) as the condition for separation. This is based on the legal principle that when the original cause cannot be implemented, a condition (shart) can stand in its place. Thus, if the waiting period elapses and the non-converting spouse still does not embrace Islam, the couple becomes separated automatically. This ruling ensures that the Muslim spouse is not perpetually bound to an unbeliever, even though the usual process of presentation and refusal cannot be carried out.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): Enemy territory where Islamic law is not enforced and Muslims have no jurisdiction.
عرض (ʿarḍ): Presenting Islam to the non-converting spouse, which is normally done before separation.
شرط (shart): A condition that substitutes for the cause when the latter is impossible to establish.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does separation not occur immediately when one spouse converts in dar al-harb?
A) Because the non-converting spouse has a right to remain married forever.
B) Because there is no Islamic authority to present Islam and effect a judicial separation.
C) Because the Muslim spouse must migrate first.
D) Because the marriage is automatically annulled without need for separation.

Key Takeaway
In dar al-harb, the waiting period substitutes for the cause of separation, so the marriage ends only after the idda elapses without the other spouse converting.

Mahr Upon Separation Due to Refusal of Islam
Ruling on dower when spouse refuses Islam and judge separates them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr when a judge separates a couple due to one spouse's refusal of Islam, and distinguish the outcomes based on which spouse refused and whether consummation occurred.

Core Idea
Wife's refusal cancels unconfirmed mahr; husband's refusal is treated as divorce.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the dower (mahr) when a Muslim couple is separated by a judge because one spouse refuses to embrace Islam. The ruling, as explained in the commentary on 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah, hinges on two factors: which spouse refuses and whether the marriage was consummated. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of mahr in cases of irtidad (apostasy) and ibā'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of ibā'
Separate the case where the wife refuses Islam from the case where the husband refuses. The ruling for each is different.
2
Determine the effect of consummation
For each scenario, check if the wife was sexually approached (mawtū'ah) or not. Mahr is fully confirmed by entry; before entry it is not yet confirmed.
Mahr is confirmed (yata'akkad) by khalwah sahihah or actual entry.
3
Apply the talil (legal reasoning)
For wife's ibā': separation is a faskh (rescission) from her side, so if the mahr is unconfirmed (before entry), it drops entirely. For husband's ibā': separation is treated as talaq, so half the specified mahr before entry, full after.
The key difference: husband's ibā' results in talaq, wife's ibā' does not.
4
Summarize the rulings
Wife's ibā': no mahr if unconsummated; full mahr if consummated. Husband's ibā': half mahr if unconsummated; full mahr if consummated.
This matches the standard Hanafi rule for talaq before and after entry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا مهر هنا إلا للموطوءة. أما في صورة إباء الزوج، فإن كانت موطوءة فكل المهر، وإن لم تكن، فنصفه؛ لأن التفريق هنا طلاق قبل الدخول. قوله: ولا مهر؛ يعني إذا كان الإباء من الزوجة، وفرق القاضي بينهما بإبائها، فإن كانت غير موطوئة فلا مهر لها؛ لأن كل فرقة من قبلها قبل أن يتأكد المهر بالوطء تسقط المهر، وإن كانت موطوءة فلها المهر بكماله لتأكد المهر بالدخول، وبعدما تأكد لا يسقط بشيء. وهذا بخلاف إباء الزوج، فإن التفريق الحاصل به طلاق، فحكمه حكم الطلاق إن كان قبل الدخول فنصف المسمى، وإلا فكله كما مر سابقاً.
And there is no dower here except for the one who has been sexually approached. As for in the case of the husband's refusal, if she has been sexually approached then the full dower, and if not then half; because the separation here is a divorce before entry. His statement: 'And no dower' means if the refusal is from the wife, and the judge separates them due to her refusal, then if she has not been sexually approached she gets no dower because every separation from her side before the dower is confirmed by sexual approach drops the dower; and if she has been sexually approached she gets the full dower because the dower is confirmed by entry and after confirmation it is not dropped by anything. And this is contrary to the husband's refusal, for the separation resulting from it is divorce, so its ruling is the ruling of divorce: if before entry then half the specified dower, otherwise all of it, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the Hanafi position on dower when a Muslim couple is separated by a judge because one spouse refuses to embrace Islam. The commentator expands on the matn, clarifying that the outcome depends on who refuses and whether consummation occurred. If the wife refuses and the judge separates them, she receives no dower if unconsummated, but full dower if consummated (since dower is confirmed by entry). If the husband refuses, the separation is treated as a divorce: half the specified dower before consummation, full dower after. The reasoning is that the separation from the husband's side is analogous to talaq, while from the wife's side it is a rescission that nullifies an unconfirmed dower.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower specified in the marriage contract; called 'musammā' when fixed.
موطوءة (mawtū'ah): A woman who has been sexually approached (entered) by her husband.
إباء (ibā'): Refusal to accept Islam; grounds for judicial separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Full dower
B) Half dower
C) No dower
D) A compensation of her choice

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, separation due to wife's refusal before consummation extinguishes the mahr, while separation due to husband's refusal is treated as talaq, entitling the wife to half the specified mahr.

Separation Due to Differing Abodes: The Condition Substitute and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on separation when one spouse converts in dar al-harb and the effect of differing abodes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on separation when one spouse converts in dar al-harb, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the nature of separation and iddah, and apply the legal principle of establishing a condition in place of a cause.

Core Idea
When one spouse converts in dar al-harb, separation is automatic after a waiting period, but whether it counts as divorce and requires iddah divides the school.

This passage examines a scenario unique to dar al-harb: a spouse converts to Islam, but there is no Muslim authority to formally present Islam to the other spouse and obtain a refusal. The Hanafi school innovates a legal mechanism: a time condition substitutes for the unavailable cause. However, internal disagreement persists on whether this separation constitutes a divorce and whether the migrant woman must observe iddah. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of legal reasoning (ta'lil) and the boundaries of ikhtilaf within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the event and the obstacle
Pinpoint the scenario: one spouse accepts Islam while both are in dar al-harb. The obstacle is the absence of Muslim jurisdiction to officially present Islam to the non-converting spouse and record a refusal.
This contrasts with dar al-Islam where a judge can decree separation.
2
Understand the legal mechanism: condition as substitute for cause
Recognize that the Hanafi jurists established the passing of a period (three menstrual cycles / three months) as a condition for separation, standing in for the impossible cause (the other spouse's refusal). This is a recognized usul principle: when the 'illa is unattainable, a condition (shart) can take its place.
This mechanism is used here and elsewhere in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the nature of the separation: divorce or rescission?
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the separation is a divorce (talaq). According to Abu Yusuf alone, it is without divorce (faskh). Trace the consequence: if talaq, it counts toward the three divorces and triggers iddah; if faskh, it does not.
The text states 'on the analogy of both their statements' referring to Sahibayn.
4
Examine the iddah dispute for the migrant woman
Abu Hanifa holds that a Muslim woman who migrates from dar al-harb observes no iddah. The Sahibayn require it. The commentator argues that the waiting period cannot be iddah because it applies even in unconsummated marriages where iddah is normally absent.
This hinges on whether the period is a condition for separation or a post-separation obligation.
5
Apply the ruling to the conversion of a Kitabi husband
If a Muslim man's Kitabi wife converts, the marriage continues until actual tabayun al-darayn occurs (e.g., one leaves the other's abode with no intent to return). Separation then occurs like a judicial decree.
This is because a Muslim may marry a Kitabiyyah, so no immediate harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان ذلك في دارهم) : أي إسلام زوج المجوسية، أو امرأة الكافر (لم تبن) الوجه في ذلك على ما في الهداية وشروحها: أنه لما أسلم أحدهما في دار الحرب مست الحاجة إلى التفريق؛ إذ المشرك لا يصلح للمسلم، ولا عرض هاهنا حتى يعرف به إباء الآخر عن الإسلام فيفرق به لانعدام يد أهل الإسلام عن أهل الحرب، وفقدان الولاية. ومن المعلوم أن إقامة الشرط مقام العلَّة عند تعذر إقامة العلة جائز، فأقمنا شرط الفرقة، وهو مضي هذه المدة مقام السبب: أي الإباء فإذا مضت هذه المدة صار مضيها حتى تحيض ثلاثاً قبل إسلام الآخر. ولو أسلم زوج الكتابية، فهي له، وتبين بتباين الدارين بمنزلة تفريق القاضي، وتكون فرقة بطلاق على قياس قولهما، وعلى قياس قول أبي يوسف له بغير طلاق. ثم هذه المدة ليست بعدة؛ لأن غير المدخول بها داخلة تحت هذا الحكم، ولو كانت عدة لم تدخل لعدم العدة في حقها. قوله: حتى تحيض؛ هذا إن كانت من ذوات الحيض، وإن كانت آيسة أو صغيرة، فالمعتبر مضي ثلاثة أشهر، فلو أسلم الآخرُ قبل مضي هذه المدة فلا بينونة ولا تفريق. قوله: فهي له؛ لعدم حرمة نكاح الكتابية بالمسلم؛ ولذا قال سابقاً: زوج المجوسية ولم يقل زوج الكافرة. قوله: وتبين؛ مضارع معروف من البينونة: أي تصير المرأة بائنة من زوجها، ويقع التفريق بينهما بتباين داريهما، إما حقيقة ككون أحدهما في دار الحرب والآخر في دار الإسلام، وإما حكماً بأن لا يكون الداخل في الدار التي دخلها على سبيل الرجوع، بل على سبيل القرار والسكنى.
And if that were in their abode: meaning the Islam of the Magian husband, or the wife of the disbeliever (does not become separated). The explanation in al-Hidayah and its commentaries: when one of them converts to Islam in dar al-harb, the need for separation arises because the polytheist is not suitable for the Muslim, and there is no presentation here by which the refusal of the other to Islam becomes known so that separation could occur, due to the lack of authority of the people of Islam over the people of war and the absence of jurisdiction. It is known that establishing a condition in place of the cause when establishing the cause is impossible is permissible, so we established the condition of separation, which is the passing of this period, in place of the cause: i.e., the refusal, so when this period passes, its passing (causes separation) until she menstruates three times before the Islam of the other. And if the husband of a Kitabiyyah converts, she remains his, and she becomes separated by the differing of the abodes like the separation of the judge, and it is a separation by divorce according to the analogy of both of their positions, and according to the analogy of Abu Yusuf's position without divorce. Then this period is not an iddah because the unconsummated wife is included under this ruling; if it were iddah, she would not be included due to the lack of iddah in her case. His statement: 'until she menstruates three times' – this is if she is of menstruating age; if she is past menopause or a minor, the measure is three months. So if the other converts before this period passes, there is no separation. His statement: 'she remains his' – due to the lack of prohibition of marrying a Kitabiyyah to a Muslim; hence previously he said 'husband of a Magian woman' and did not say 'husband of a disbelieving woman'. His statement: 'and she becomes separated' – it is a known verb from baynunah: meaning the woman becomes bain from her husband, and separation occurs between them due to the differing of their abodes, either literally as one being in dar al-harb and the other in dar al-Islam, or constructively as the entrant not intending return but settlement.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a dispute-explaining expansion within the Hanafi school. It covers the scenario when one spouse converts to Islam in dar al-harb (the abode of war). Since there is no Muslim authority to present Islam to the other spouse and obtain a refusal, the condition for separation is the passage of time: if the other spouse does not convert within the period of three menstrual cycles (or three months for non-menstruating women), separation automatically occurs. This separation is treated as a divorce according to both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, but as a separation without divorce according to Abu Yusuf's alternative view. A key intra-Hanafi dispute concerns the iddah: Abu Hanifa holds that a Muslim woman who migrates from dar al-harb does not observe iddah, while the Sahibayn differ. Additionally, if a Muslim marries a Kitabiyyah in dar al-harb and then leaves, the marriage remains valid until the abodes differ (tabayun al-darayn), which effects a separation akin to a judge's decree. The commentator clarifies that the waiting period is not an iddah, as it applies even to unconsummated marriages where iddah is not normally required.

Key Terms
تباين الدارين (tabāyun al-dārayn): The legal separation caused by one spouse living in dar al-Islam and the other in dar al-harb with no intention of returning.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or separation during which a woman cannot remarry; here debated for the migrant woman.
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): A territory not under Muslim rule where non-Muslims hold authority.
إقامة الشرط مقام العلة (iqāmat al-sharṭ maqām al-ʿilla): The legal principle of substituting a condition for a cause when the cause cannot be realized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the waiting period after conversion in dar al-harb is NOT an iddah because:
A) It only applies to consummated marriages.
B) It applies even to unconsummated marriages, where iddah is not required.
C) The woman is considered divorced immediately.
D) The period is for the husband to pronounce talaq.

Key Takeaway
In dar al-harb, the passing of a time condition substitutes for the unavailable refusal cause, effecting separation, but whether that separation is a divorce and requires iddah remains an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Difference of Abodes and Its Effect on Marriage: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on tabāyun al-dārayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that tabāyun al-dārayn annuls marriage and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith of Zaynab.

Core Idea
Tabāyun al-dārayn causes separation because harmony is impossible; captivity alone does not.

In this lesson, we examine a classical inter-madhhab dispute: does a difference of abodes—one spouse in Dār al-Islām, the other in Dār al-Ḥarb—automatically dissolve a marriage? The Hanafi school argues yes, while the Shafi'i school argues no, citing the incident of Zaynab bint Muhammad. We will unpack the Hanafi proof, the counter-evidence, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school? Does tabāyun al-dārayn annul the marriage? If so, is it considered a divorce or an annulment (faskh)?
The text states the woman becomes ba'in, and there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether it is talaq or faskh.
2
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
What is the underlying reason ('illa) for the Hanafi ruling? How does the text explain why tabāyun causes separation but captivity does not?
The 'illa is that 'harmony of interests is not possible' when spouses live in different abodes.
3
Present the Shafi'i objection
What evidence does Imam al-Shafi'i use to argue that tabāyun does not annul the marriage? Identify the specific hadith and how it is interpreted.
The hadith of Zaynab and Abu al-As: the Prophet returned her to him 'with the first marriage' after hijra.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the Shafi'i argument? What is the alternative interpretation of the hadith?
Authentic narrations show it was a new marriage (nikāḥ jadīd), not a continuation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two positions? In a real scenario, what consequence does this dispute have?
E.g., a Muslim woman who migrates from Dār al-Ḥarb: Shafi'i says marriage continues, Hanafi says it is automatically annulled.

بالفتح - : أي لا تبين المرأة بمجرد السبي بـيـد المسلمين، والوجه في هذا الباب: أن بالتباين في الدارين لا تنتظم المصالح، فينقطع النكاح، والسبي الواجب ملك الرقبة، وهو لا ينافي في النكاح ابتداء، كما إذا زوج أمته لغيره، فكذلك بقاء. فإن قلت: قد ورد أن زينب بنت النبي هاجرت من مكة إلى المدينة، وخلفت زوجها أبا العاص بن الربيع بمكة، فلما وصل إلى المدينة ردها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالنكاح الأول، فلو كان تباين الدارين موجباً للفرقة لَمَّا صح ذلك، وبهذا استند الشافعي في أن التباين لا يوجب الفرقة. قلت: قد صح في «جامع التّرْمِذِي» و «سنن ابن ماجة» و«مسند أحمد» وغيرها: أنه ردها رسول الله الله عليه بنكاح جديد، ومعنى ما روى بالنكاح الأول أنه ردَّها عليه بمثل النكاح الأوّل، ولم يحدث شيئا من زيادة في المهر وغيرها. فإن قلت: قوله عند ذكر المحرمات والمحصنات من النساء: أي ذوات الأزواج: إلَّا مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَنُكُمْ، يدلُّ بإطلاقه على أن السبي يوجب الفرقة، كما قال به الشافعي. قلت: هو محمول على ما إذا سبيت المرأة فقط، فإنّه حينئذ يقع الفرقة؛ لوجود تباين الدارين حكماً. كذا في «البناية».
And if the husband of a Kitabiyya (Scripture woman) becomes Muslim, she remains his wife, and she becomes separated (ba'in) by the difference of the two abodes (tabāyun al-dārayn), like a judge's separation. And the separation is through divorce according to the analogy of their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) statement, and according to the analogy of Abu Yusuf's statement it is without divorce. Then this period is not an 'iddah because the non-consummated woman is included under this ruling; if it were an 'iddah she would not be included due to no 'iddah in her case. And is the 'iddah obligatory after the passing of this period? If the woman is a Harbiyya, then no, because there is no 'iddah for a Harbiyya. Thus in al-Nahr. 'Not by captivity'—with fatḥa: meaning the woman does not become separated by mere captivity at the hands of Muslims. And the reasoning in this chapter is that by difference of abodes harmony is not possible, so marriage is severed; while captivity necessitates ownership of the person, and it is not incompatible with marriage initially, just as if he married his slave-woman to someone else, so likewise continuance. If you say: it has been reported that Zaynab bint the Prophet emigrated from Mecca to Medina, leaving her husband Abū al-Āṣ ibn al-Rabī' in Mecca. When he came to Medina, the Messenger of Allah ﷺ returned her to him with the first marriage. So if difference of abodes necessitated separation, that would not be correct, and the Shāfi'ī relies on this that difference of abodes does not necessitate separation. I say: it is authentically reported in Jāmi' al-Tirmidhī, Sunan Ibn Mājah, Musnad Aḥmad and others that he returned her to him with a new marriage. And the meaning of what was narrated as 'with the first marriage' is that he returned her to him with a marriage like the first, not increasing anything in the dowry or otherwise. If you say: His statement when mentioning the forbidden women and the muhşanāt (chaste women) i.e. those with husbands: 'except what your right hands possess' (Qur'an 4:24), indicates by its generality that captivity necessitates separation, as the Shāfi'ī said. I say: it is restricted to the case when only the woman is captured; then separation occurs due to the existence of difference of abodes figuratively. Thus in al-Bināyah.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute from the commentary on Sharh al-Wiqāyah. The author explains the Hanafi ruling that a Muslim man's marriage to a Kitābiyyah woman remains valid when he embraces Islam, but the marriage ends if there is a difference of abodes (tabāyun al-dārayn) — i.e., one spouse remains in Dār al-Ḥarb and the other is in Dār al-Islām, whether literally or effectively. The separation is treated like a judge's dissolution (tafrīq). Within the Hanafi school, there is a dispute: according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf (the Sāḥibayn), the separation is considered a divorce (ṭalāq), whereas according to another narration from Abu Yusuf, it is without divorce (i.e., faskh). The passage also clarifies that the period of waiting (if any) is not an 'iddah proper, because the ruling applies even to non-consummated marriages. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that even a Muslim woman emigrating from Dār al-Ḥarb does not observe 'iddah. The key point of contention with Imam al-Shāfi'ī is: Shāfi'ī argues that tabāyun al-dārayn does not cause separation, based on the incident of Zaynab bint Muhammad (ﷺ), who emigrated to Medina while her husband remained in Mecca, and the Prophet (ﷺ) returned her to him upon his Islam. The Hanafi response is that authentic narrations show the Prophet (ﷺ) married her with a new contract (nikāḥ jadīd), not the original one. Thus, tabāyun al-dārayn does cause separation, and the new marriage after emigration confirms that. As for the Qur'anic verse about captive women (illā mā malakat aymānukum) which Shāfi'ī uses to argue that captivity alone ends the marriage, the Hanafis qualify it: it applies only when the woman is captured alone, because then there is effective tabāyun. But if both are captured together, no separation occurs. This explains that the marriage ends due to the separation of abodes, not simply by captivity.

Key Terms
تباين الدارين (tabāyun al-dārayn): Difference of abodes: one spouse residing in Dār al-Ḥarb (land of war) and the other in Dār al-Islām (land of Islam), either literally or effectively (e.g., permanent migration), causing the marriage to dissolve.
سبي (saby): Captivity; taking a free person as a captive of war. The text argues it does not by itself annul marriage because it only transfers ownership of the person, which is compatible with marriage.
بينونة (bainūna): Final separation between spouses; the marriage becomes irrevocably dissolved (ba'in), not a revocable divorce (raj'i).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does mere captivity (saby) of a married woman by Muslim forces automatically end her marriage?
A) Yes, based on Qur'an 4:24.
B) No, because captivity only transfers ownership, which does not invalidate marriage.
C) Yes, because the husband is in Dar al-Harb.
D) No, but only if the husband is also captured.

Key Takeaway
Tabāyun al-dārayn annuls marriage due to loss of harmony; captivity alone does not, but if only the woman is captured, effective tabāyun occurs.

The Hanafi Position on Capture (Sabi) and Separation of Marriage
Explains that mere capture does not end marriage, but change of abode does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that mere capture does not dissolve marriage, and distinguish it from divergence of abodes as the actual cause.

Core Idea
Capture alone does not end a marriage; only divergence of abodes (tabayun al-darayn) severs it.

This passage presents a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the effect of capture (sabi) on an existing marriage. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between capture, which changes ownership but does not affect the marriage bond, and divergence of abodes, which makes the continuation of marriage impracticable. The commentator defends the Hanafi position against two Shafi'i proofs from hadith and Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Start by noting the passage's opening statement: the woman does not become separated by mere capture by Muslims. This is the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The passage explains the rationale: marriage interests require a shared abode. Divergence of dar (being in different territories) disrupts that, so it causes separation. Capture merely changes ownership, which does not contradict marriage—similar to a master marrying his own slave-woman to another.
The analogy to milk al-yamin (right hand ownership) is crucial: ownership does not preclude marriage.
3
Analyze the first Shafi'i proof (Zaynab hadith) and the Hanafi response
Shafi'i argues that the Prophet (SAW) returned Zaynab to Abu al-As with the first marriage despite migration, implying divergence of dar does not cause separation. The Hanafi response clarifies that the correct narration says it was a new marriage, so the first marriage had ended due to divergence when she migrated.
Thus, this hadith does not refute but supports the Hanafi position.
4
Analyze the second Shafi'i proof (Quran 4:24) and the Hanafi response
Shafi'i cites the verse 'except what your right hands possess' as general (amm) permission to have intercourse with captured married women, implying capture dissolves marriage. The Hanafi response restricts (muqayyad) this to cases where the woman alone is captured and brought to dar al-Islam, creating a juristic divergence of dar.
If both are captured together, divergence does not occur, so the marriage remains.
5
Apply the principle to three scenarios
The passage branches into three cases: one spouse comes to dar al-Islam as Muslim or captive (divergence found, separation), both captured together (no divergence, no separation). This confirms the principle that capture alone is not the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لا بالسبي؛ - بالفتح - : أي لا تبين المرأة بمجرد السبي بيد المسلمين، والوجه في هذا الباب : أن بالتباين في الدارين لا تنتظم المصالح، فينقطع النكاح، والسبي الواجب ملك الرقبة، وهو لا ينافي في النكاح ابتداء، كما إذا زوج أمته لغيره ، فكذلك بقاء. فإن قلت : قد ورد أن زينب بنت النبي هاجرت من مكة إلى المدينة، وخلفت زوجها أبا العاص بن الربيع بمكة، فلما وصل إلى المدينة ردها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالنكاح الأول، فلو كان تباين الدارين موجباً للفرقة لَمَّا صح ذلك، وبهذا استند الشافعي في أن التباين لا يوجب الفرقة. قلت : قد صح في «جامع الترمذي» و «سنن ابن ماجة» و«مسند أحمد» وغيرها : أنه ردها رسول الله الله عليه بنكاح جديد، ومعنى ما روى بالنكاح الأول أنه ردَّها عليه بمثل النكاح الأوّل ، ولم يحدث شيئا من زيادة في المهر وغيرها. فإن قلت : قوله عند ذكر المحرمات والمحصنات من النساء : أي ذوات الأزواج : إلَّا مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَنُكُمْ ، يدلُّ بإطلاقه على أن السبي يوجب الفرقة، كما قال به الشافعي . قلت : هو محمول على ما إذا سبيت المرأة فقط، فإنّه حينئذ يقع الفرقة ؛ لوجود تباين الدارين حكماً. كذا في «البناية» قوله : فلو؛ تفريع على ما ذُكِرَ من أن التباين يوجب الفرقة دون السبي، هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي له عكسه، ففي صورة خروج أحدهما مسلماً إلينا وُجد التباين، وفي إخراج أحدهما مسبياً أيضاً وجد التباين وفي صورة سبيهما معاً لم يوجد إلا السبي.
His statement: 'Not by capture' - with fatha on the ba - means: the woman does not become separated by mere capture by the Muslims. The principle in this chapter is that by divergence of abodes (tabayun al-darayn) interests are not maintained, so marriage is severed. Capture necessitates ownership of the person, and that does not contradict marriage initially, just as if he marries his slave-woman to someone else, so likewise in continuance. If you say: It has been reported that Zaynab, the daughter of the Prophet, migrated from Makkah to Madinah, leaving her husband Abu al-As ibn al-Rabi' in Makkah. When he reached Madinah, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) returned her to him with the first marriage. If divergence of abodes necessitated separation, that would not be sound. With this, al-Shafi'i relied that divergence does not necessitate separation. I say: It is established in Jami' al-Tirmidhi, Sunan Ibn Majah, Musnad Ahmad, and others, that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) returned her to him with a new marriage. The meaning of what is reported as 'the first marriage' is that he returned her to him with a marriage like the first, without increasing the dowry or anything else. If you say: His statement when mentioning prohibited women and muhsanat (chaste women) meaning those with husbands: 'Except what your right hands possess' (4:24) indicates by its generality that capture necessitates separation, as al-Shafi'i said. I say: It is interpreted as when the woman alone is captured, for then separation occurs due to the existence of divergence of abodes juristically. This is in al-Binayah. His statement: 'So if' - a branching from what was mentioned, that divergence necessitates separation, not capture. This is our position, and according to al-Shafi'i the opposite. So in the case of one of them coming out to us as a Muslim, divergence is found; in the case of one of them being taken out as a captive, divergence is also found; and in the case of both being captured together, nothing but capture is found.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether mere capture (sabi) of a spouse by Muslims causes the marriage to end. The Hanafi position is that it does not; only divergence of abodes (tabayun al-darayn) does. The commentator first defines 'sabi' and explains the underlying principle: the interests of marriage are not maintained when husband and wife are in different territories, hence separation, but capture merely changes ownership of the person, which does not contradict the marriage bond. He then addresses two objections from the Shafi'i school. First, the hadith of Zaynab bint Muhammad (RA) and her husband Abu al-As: if divergence of dar causes separation, how could the Prophet (SAW) reunite them? The Hanafi response clarifies that the hadith quoted by Shafi'i is weak; the correct narration states the Prophet (SAW) returned her with a new marriage, not the original one. Thus, the hadith does not support the claim that divergence does not cause separation. Second, the Shafi'i proof from the Quranic verse 'except what your right hands possess' (4:24), which seems to permit intercourse with captured married women, implying capture ends their marriage. The Hanafi reply restricts this to cases where the woman is captured alone and brought to dar al-Islam, resulting in a juristic divergence of dar (since she is now in Muslim territory and her husband remains in dar al-harb). If both are captured together, no separation occurs. The passage then branches (falu) into three specific cases: if one spouse comes to dar al-Islam as a Muslim or is captured, separation occurs due to divergence of dar; if both are captured together, no separation. This confirms the principle.

Key Terms
سبي (sabi): Captivity; the act of taking someone as a captive in war, resulting in ownership of the person.
تباين الدارين (tabayun al-darayn): Divergence of abodes; when spouses reside in different territories (dar al-Islam vs. dar al-harb) such that there is no safe conduct or free movement between them.
ملك اليمين (milk al-yamin): Ownership by right hand; refers to ownership of slaves or captives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if both a husband and wife are captured together by Muslims and brought to dar al-Islam, does their marriage end?
A) Yes, because capture itself dissolves the marriage.
B) No, because divergence of dar (tabayun al-darayn) did not occur since both are now in dar al-Islam.
C) Yes, because they are now owned by new masters.
D) No, but only if they are both Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that capture (sabi) does not dissolve marriage; only divergence of abodes (tabayun al-darayn) does, based on the principle that ownership is compatible with marriage and the correct reading of the hadith and Quran.

Ruling on a Migrant Woman's Iddah: No Waiting Period Unless Pregnant
States the ruling on a migrant woman's iddah with reasoning and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a migrant woman's iddah, explain the reasoning (ta'lil) that no iddah is required for non-pregnant women, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement with the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A migrant woman's marriage ends immediately; she may remarry at once — no iddah unless pregnant.

This passage from a commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a woman who migrates from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam. The ruling distinguishes between pregnant and non-pregnant women and involves an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two principal disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding the reasoning (ta'lil) behind the ruling is essential for applying it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the migration of a woman from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam with the intention of permanent settlement. The ruling: her marriage ends immediately (she becomes bāʾin), and she does not need to observe iddah before remarrying, unless she is pregnant.
The term 'migration' here excludes temporary visits; it requires a change of domicile.
2
Locate the reasoning (ta'lil)
The commentator provides the legal reasoning: iddah is ordained to honor the husband's authority (ʿizzat al-milk) over the marriage. A harbi husband has no recognized authority in dar al-Islam, so the purpose of iddah is absent. Therefore, no iddah is required.
This is a maʿnā-based (rational) proof, not a textual one.
3
Examine the exception for pregnancy
The pregnant woman must wait until delivery before remarrying. This is because her pregnancy is attributed to the harbi husband and must be clarified to avoid confusion of lineage. This is the mufta bihi position (zahir al-riwayah).
An alternative weaker narration (from Hasan ibn Ziyād) allows immediate marriage but prohibits consummation until delivery.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) disagreed with Abu Hanifa. They argued that the separation occurs within dar al-Islam, so Islamic rules apply, including iddah. Thus, they required iddah for all migrant women, pregnant or not.
The Sahibayn's view is not the mufta bihi; Abu Hanifa's position is relied upon in fatwa.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and mufta bihi
The text clearly presents Abu Hanifa's view as the main ruling, and the Sahibayn's view is noted but not adopted. For the pregnant woman, the zahir al-riwayah (wait until delivery) is the operative position, though a minority later followed the alternative narration preferred by al-Aqṭaʿ.
Authority weight: This is from the core Hanafi works (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومن هاجرت ؛ أي تركت دار الحرب ووصلت إلى دار الإسلام على عزم الإقامة، وعدم العود ؛ وذلك بأن تخرج مسلمة أو ذمية. كذا في «البحر». هذه المسألة وإن كانت داخلة فيما قبلها لكن كان المقصود سابقاً مجرد وقوع الفرقة بتباين الدارين بخروج أحد الزوجين إلينا مهاجراً ، والمقصود هاهنا بيان عدم العدة على المرأة المهاجرة. قوله : بلا عدة ؛ هذا عنده ، وخالفاه ظناً منها أن الفرقة وقعت بعد الدخول في دار الإسلام فيلزمها أحكام الإسلام، ومنها: العدة. وله : إن العدة آخر النكاح المتقدّم إظهاراً لعزّة ملك الزوج، ولا عزّة لملك الحربي فلا عدة، فلها أن تزوج بمجرد دخولها في دار الإسلام. قوله : إلا الحامل ؛ ظاهره أن عليها العدّة ، وهو مدة وضع الحمل، فلا يجوز لها النكاح قبل وضع الحمل ؛ وذلك لأن حملها ثابت النسب، فيؤثر في منع النكاح احتياطاً، وهذا هو ظاهر الرواية. وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له أنه يصح نكاحها قبل الوضع، لكن لا يقربها زوجها حتى تضع كالحبلى من الزنا، ورجحها الأقطع شارح «مختصر القدوري». كذا في «النهر»
His statement: 'And whoever migrates to us' – i.e., she leaves dar al-harb and arrives in dar al-Islam with the intention of settling and not returning; that can be as a Muslim or a dhimmi. This is in al-Bahr. This issue, although included in what came before, the earlier intent was merely the occurrence of separation by divergence of dar due to one spouse coming out to us as a migrant, while the intent here is to explain the absence of iddah for the migrant woman. His statement: 'without iddah' – this is his (Abu Hanifa's) view, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differed with him, thinking that the separation occurred after entering dar al-Islam, so the rules of Islam apply to her, including iddah. He (Abu Hanifa) said: iddah is the last part of the previous marriage to show the honor of the husband's authority, and there is no honor for the authority of a harbi, so no iddah. Thus, she may marry immediately upon entering dar al-Islam. His statement: 'except the pregnant' – its apparent meaning is that she must observe iddah, which is the period until delivery, so she cannot marry before delivery. This is because her pregnancy is established in lineage, and it prevents marriage as a precaution. This is the apparent position (zahir al-riwayah). In a narration from Hasan from Abu Hanifa, he held that her marriage before delivery is valid, but her husband should not consummate until she delivers, like the case of a pregnant woman from adultery. This was preferred by al-Aqta', the commentator of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. This is in al-Nahr.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The ruling: when a woman migrates from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam, her marriage ends immediately upon her arrival, and she is not required to observe an iddah (waiting period) before remarrying, unless she is pregnant. The commentator first defines 'migrates' as leaving dar al-harb with the intention of permanent settlement, whether she is Muslim or dhimmi. He notes that although the same principle of separation due to divergence of dar applies, the focus here is specifically on the iddah ruling. The reason for no iddah is that iddah is meant to honor the husband's authority (izzat al-milk), but a harbi husband has no recognized authority in dar al-Islam. Thus, the woman may remarry immediately. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. However, his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differed: they argued that since the separation occurred within dar al-Islam, Islamic rules apply, including iddah. The commentary does not settle this dispute but presents Abu Hanifa's argument. For the exception of pregnancy, the apparent ruling (zahir al-riwayah) is that she must wait until delivery to marry, because the pregnancy is attributed to the harbi husband and to avoid confusion of lineage. An alternative narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa holds that her marriage is valid before delivery but consummation is prohibited until delivery, analogously to a pregnant woman from adultery. This weaker narration was favored by some later scholars like al-Aqta'.

Key Terms
هاجرت (hajarat): Migrated from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam with the intention of permanent settlement, not temporary visit.
عدة (iddah): Waiting period after separation to determine pregnancy and honor the husband's authority.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent and main transmitted ruling from the school, as opposed to a rare or weak narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwayah), when a woman migrates from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam and is pregnant, what is her ruling?
A) She may marry immediately upon arrival, but cannot consummate until delivery.
B) She must wait until delivery to marry.
C) She must observe a 4-month 10-day iddah.
D) She can marry immediately without any restrictions.

Key Takeaway
A migrant woman from dar al-harb to dar al-Islam does not observe iddah unless she is pregnant; the harbi husband's lack of authority nullifies the basis for iddah.

Apostasy of a Spouse is Faskh, Not Talaq
Clarifies that apostasy annuls marriage as faskh, not talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between faskh and talaq in the context of apostasy and apply the ruling that remarriage is permissible without tahlil.

Core Idea
Apostasy annuls marriage as faskh, not talaq — remarriage is possible without tahlil even after repeated apostasy.

This lesson clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi nikah law: the difference between faskh (annulment) and talaq (divorce). When a spouse leaves Islam, the marriage is instantly annulled, but this annulment does not count as a divorce. Therefore, if the apostate returns to Islam and wishes to remarry the same spouse, no intermediate marriage (tahlil) is required. This ruling has significant practical implications for couples facing apostasy and subsequent return to faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Read the passage and note that the commentator explicitly states 'faskh' (annulment) and contrasts it with 'talaq' (divorce). Understand that the ruling is that apostasy results in faskh, not talaq.
The term 'faskh' is key; it means the marriage is dissolved by the act itself, not by a pronouncement.
2
Distinguish Faskh from Talaq
Explain the legal difference: talaq is a revocable or irrevocable dissolution initiated by the husband (or wife by delegation), while faskh is an automatic annulment due to a cause (here, apostasy). Faskh does not count toward the three-talaq limit.
This distinction is crucial because it affects the ability to remarry without tahlil.
3
Apply to the Multiple Apostasy Scenario
Consider the example given: a man apostatizes repeatedly, returns to Islam each time, and renews the marriage. According to the Hanafi ruling, each time the marriage is annulled and can be renewed without tahlil because the separation is faskh, not a third talaq.
This shows the practical mercy of the ruling—it does not create an insurmountable barrier to remarriage.
4
Note the Source Authority
The passage references 'al-Khaniyya' (al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya), a later Hanafi fatwa collection. This indicates the ruling is from the fatawa mutakhkhirin (later opinions), confirming its operative status.
Knowing the authority weight helps assess reliability in the Hanafi hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فسخ ؛ أي ليس بطلاق، فلو ارتد مراراً وجدد الإسلام كل مرة، وجدد النكاح تحلُّ لامرأته من غير الحاجة إلى التحليل. كذا في «الخانية».
His statement: 'annulment' – i.e., it is not divorce. So if he apostatizes repeatedly and renews Islam each time, and renews the marriage, she becomes permissible to his wife without the need for tahlil (an intermediate marriage). This is in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This is a ruling statement clarifying the legal effect of apostasy on marriage. The commentator explains that when a spouse apostatizes, the marriage is annulled (faskh), not dissolved by divorce (talaq). The practical consequence is significant: if a husband apostatizes and then returns to Islam, he can remarry his wife without her having to marry another man and divorce him (tahlil). This is because the separation is not a complete release (talaq) that makes her prohibited to him until she marries another. The ruling applies even if the apostasy and return to Islam happen multiple times; each time, the marriage can be renewed without tahlil. This is based on the principle that apostasy severs the marriage contract by its very nature, not by a revocable divorce. The source is al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya, a standard Hanafi reference.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or dissolution of a contract; here, of marriage due to apostasy, not by the pronouncement of talaq.
تحليل (tahlil): An intermediate marriage: a woman who has been divorced thrice must marry another man and be divorced by him before she can remarry her first husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a husband apostatizes and then returns to Islam, can he remarry his wife without her having to marry another man first?
A) Yes, because apostasy is faskh, not talaq.
B) No, because apostasy is equivalent to triple talaq.
C) Yes, but only if he did not apostatize more than once.
D) No, she must observe iddah first.

Key Takeaway
Apostasy annuls marriage as faskh, not talaq; this allows remarriage without tahlil even after repeated apostasy and return.

Ruling on the Migrant Woman's 'Iddah and Exception for Pregnancy
Explains the rule and rationale for no 'iddah for migrant woman, except pregnant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'iddah for a woman migrating from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam and explain the rationale (talil) for the absence of 'iddah, including the exception for pregnancy.

Core Idea
A husband in Dar al-Harb has no honor (izzah) in Islamic law, so his wife's 'iddah is void upon migration.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah tackles a specific scenario: a woman leaves Dar al-Harb for Dar al-Islam, leaving her husband behind. Does she need to observe 'iddah before remarrying? The Hanafi answer is surprising: no, because 'iddah honors the husband's authority, and a harbi husband has no such honor. Pregnancy is a key exception. Let's walk through the reasoning and the scholarly disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The woman migrates from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam with intention to settle. She becomes ba'in immediately upon entry. The ruling: no 'iddah, so she may remarry instantly. Exception: if pregnant, she must wait until delivery.
This ruling applies whether she is Muslim or dhimmi.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is not a specific text but a rationale (talil): 'iddah is to manifest the honor of the husband's ownership (izzah al-milk). A husband in Dar al-Harb has no honor in Islamic law, so the purpose of 'iddah is absent.
This is a teleological argument based on the wisdom behind 'iddah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The link: 'iddah is legislated for izzah al-milk. When milk has no izzah (as in Dar al-Harb), the ruling ( 'iddah) falls away. Thus no 'iddah is required.
This reasoning is specific to Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn do not accept it.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i hold that 'iddah is required because the separation occurs in Dar al-Islam, so Islamic rulings apply. The Hanafi response: the rationale of 'iddah (husband's honor) is absent regardless of location.
This is a classic example of ta'lil (rationale-based reasoning) overriding a general rule.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf for the pregnant woman
Zahir al-riwayah: pregnant migrant must wait until delivery. But there is a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa that marriage before delivery is valid, though consummation is prohibited until delivery, similar to a zina pregnancy. Al-Aqta' preferred this view.
The mufta bihi position is the zahir al-riwayah (must wait), but the alternative has traction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هاجرت إلينا بانت بلا عدة إلا الحامل [١] قوله : ومن هاجرت ؛ أي تركت دار الحرب ووصلت إلى دار الإسلام على عزم الإقامة، وعدم العود ؛ وذلك بأن تخرج مسلمة أو ذمية. كذا في «البحر». هذه المسألة وإن كانت داخلة فيما قبلها لكن كان المقصود سابقاً مجرد وقوع الفرقة بتباين الدارين بخروج أحد الزوجين إلينا مهاجراً ، والمقصود هاهنا بيان عدم العدة على المرأة المهاجرة. [٢]قوله : بلا عدة ؛ هذا عنده ، وخالفاه ظناً منها أن الفرقة وقعت بعد الدخول في دار الإسلام فيلزمها أحكام الإسلام، ومنها: العدة. وله : إن العدة آخر النكاح المتقدّم إظهاراً لعزّة ملك الزوج، ولا عزّة لملك الحربي فلا عدة، فلها أن تزوج بمجرد دخولها في دار الإسلام. [٣] قوله : إلا الحامل ؛ ظاهره أن عليها العدّة ، وهو مدة وضع الحمل، فلا يجوز لها النكاح قبل وضع الحمل ؛ وذلك لأن حملها ثابت النسب، فيؤثر في منع النكاح احتياطاً، وهذا هو ظاهر الرواية. وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له أنه يصح نكاحها قبل الوضع، لكن لا يقربها زوجها حتى تضع كالحبلى من الزنا، ورجحها الأقطع شارح «مختصر القدوري». كذا في «النهر»
And whoever emigrates to us becomes separated without any waiting period except the pregnant woman. [1] His statement: 'And whoever emigrates to us' means she left the Abode of War and arrived at the Abode of Islam with the intention to settle and not return; this applies whether she leaves as a Muslim or a dhimmi. Thus in al-Bahr. This issue, though included in what preceded, the earlier purpose was simply the occurrence of separation by difference of the two abodes due to one of the spouses emigrating to us, whereas the purpose here is to explain the absence of 'iddah for the migrant woman. [2] His statement: 'without any waiting period' is his (Abu Hanifa's) view, and the two companions differed from him, thinking that the separation occurred after entering the Abode of Islam, so the rulings of Islam apply to her, including 'iddah. For him (Abu Hanifa): 'iddah is the conclusion of the previous marriage to manifest the honor (izzah) of the husband's ownership, and the husband's ownership in the Abode of War has no honor, so there is no 'iddah. Thus she may marry as soon as she enters the Abode of Islam. [3] His statement: 'except the pregnant woman' means that apparently she must observe 'iddah, which is the period until she gives birth, so she is not permitted to marry before delivery; because her pregnancy is established in lineage, so it prevents marriage as a precaution. This is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, her marriage before delivery is valid, but her husband may not approach her until she delivers, like a pregnant woman from zina. Al-Aqta', commentator of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, preferred this. Thus in al-Nahr.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It addresses the case of a woman who migrates from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam—whether she is Muslim or dhimmi—and leaves her husband behind. The ruling is that she becomes separated (ba'in) immediately upon entering Dar al-Islam without any 'iddah, except if she is pregnant. The commentator first defines the term 'hajara' as leaving Dar al-Harb with the intention of permanent settlement in Dar al-Islam. He clarifies that although this issue was touched upon earlier in the context of separation due to different abodes, here the focus is specifically on the absence of 'iddah for the migrant woman. Then he explains the rationale: Abu Hanifa holds that 'iddah is meant to honor the husband's marital authority (izzah al-milk). Since a husband in Dar al-Harb has no such honor in the eyes of Islamic law, no 'iddah is required. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that since separation occurs in Dar al-Islam, Islamic rulings including 'iddah apply. The commentator then handles the exception for pregnancy: according to the clear narration (zahir al-riwayah), a pregnant migrant woman must wait until she delivers before remarrying, because the child's lineage is established and requires precaution. However, there is a weak narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa that her marriage before delivery is valid but consummation is prohibited until delivery, similar to a woman pregnant from zina. Al-Aqta' preferred this latter view. The passage concludes with reference to al-Nahr al-Fa'iq.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after separation from her husband before remarrying, meant to confirm the absence of pregnancy and honor the husband's ownership.
عزّة ملك الزوج (izzah al-milk al-zawj): The honor/strength of the husband's marital authority, which 'iddah is meant to manifest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the marriage was invalid in Dar al-Harb
B) Because the husband's ownership in Dar al-Harb has no honor (izzah)
C) Because she is now under Muslim rule and exempt
D) Because 'iddah is only required for divorce, not separation by migration

Key Takeaway
A woman migrating from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam does not observe 'iddah because the husband's ownership lacks honor (izzah), except if she is pregnant, in which case she must wait until delivery per the preferred narration.

Apostasy by Spouses: Faskh, Mahr, and Istihsan
Ruling on apostasy's effect on marriage with proofs and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that apostasy by a spouse is an immediate annulment (faskh), explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and apply the rules of mahr and the istihsan for joint apostasy.

Core Idea
Apostasy by a spouse is an immediate annulment (faskh), not a talaq; the marriage survives by istihsan if both return to Islam together.

This lesson examines the ruling on apostasy by one or both spouses according to the Hanafi school. The key point is that apostasy is considered an immediate annulment (faskh) of the marriage, not a revocable divorce (talaq). This has significant consequences for mahr, 'iddah, and the possibility of remarriage. The passage also introduces the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Imam Muhammad and the application of istihsan when both spouses apostatize and return together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of apostasy as annulment
The text states that apostasy is a faskh (annulment) that takes effect immediately without requiring a judge's ruling or waiting for 'iddah to expire. Recognize that this is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
Faskh is different from talaq; it does not count towards the three divorces.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the husband's apostasy is a talaq, analogizing to the case of refusing Islam. Trace Abu Hanifa's distinction: apostasy contradicts the marriage, while talaq lifts it without contradiction.
The practical difference: if talaq, the husband can reclaim during 'iddah; if faskh, no return.
3
Apply the mahr rules
For a consummated wife: full mahr whether husband or wife apostatizes. For a non-consummated wife: half mahr if husband apostatizes, nothing if wife apostatizes (because separation came from her before mahr was confirmed). Also understand 'iddah maintenance follows the same pattern.
The mahr is confirmed at marriage but can be reduced if separation is prior to consummation due to the wife's action.
4
Analyze the istihsan for joint apostasy
If both spouses apostatize together and then both return to Islam (actually or virtually), the marriage remains valid by istihsan, overriding the qiyas that would declare it void. The historical precedent of Banu Hanifah supports this.
Qiyas would require renewal of marriage, but istihsan preserves it to avoid hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله : عاجل ؛ أي بلا توقف على قضاء القاضي، ولا توقف على مضي عدة في المدخولة، وهذا كله عنده وعن أبي يوسف، وعند محمد ه ارتداده طلاق، وارتدادها فسخ، قياساً على مسألة الإباء عن الإسلام، والفرق لأبي حنيفة : أن الردّة منافية للنكاح والطلاق رافع للنكاح لا منافٍ، فتعذَّرَ جعلُهُ طلاقاً بخلاف الإباء. [٢] قوله : ولا شيء ؛ عليه لا المهر ولا النفقة في العدّة ؛ لعدم وجوب العدّة على غير المدخول بها، وأما المدخولةُ فلها نفقة العدة إن ارتد كالمهر، وإن ارتدت فلها المهر لا النفقة، والعدّة أيضاً واجبة فيها بالحيض أو بالأشهر أو بوضع الحمل. كذا في «البحر». [٣] قوله : لو ارتدت ؛ أي غير الموطوءة ؛ لأن الفرقة جاءت من قبلها قبل تأكد المهر، فيسقط المهر. [٤] قوله : ثم أسلما ؛ معاً معيّةً حقيقية بأن تكلّما معاً بكلمة الإسلام أو حكميّة بأن أسلما ولم يُعْلَمْ سَبْقُ أحدهما، والقياس يقتضي فسادُ النكاح ؛ لوجود ارتداد مناف للنكاح، وإنا حكمنا بعدم الفسادِ استحسانا، واستشهدوا لذلك بما روي أن بني حنيفة ارتدوا ثم أسلموا في زمان أبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه ولم يؤمروا بتجديد الأنكحة.
And apostasy of each of them is an annulment (faskh) immediate. Then for the woman who was consummated, her full mahr; for the one not consummated, half of it if he apostatizes, and nothing if she apostatizes. The marriage remains if both apostatize together and then both become Muslim, and it becomes void if one becomes Muslim before the other. [1] His statement: 'immediate' means without waiting for a judge's ruling, and without waiting for the expiry of 'iddah in the case of a consummated wife. This is all his (Abu Hanifa's) view and according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, his apostasy is talaq, and her apostasy is faskh, by analogy to the issue of refusing Islam. The distinction for Abu Hanifa: apostasy contradicts (munafin) the marriage, whereas talaq lifts (rafi') the marriage but does not contradict it. Therefore it is impossible to make it talaq, unlike refusing Islam. [2] His statement: 'and nothing' means neither mahr nor 'iddah maintenance, because 'iddah is not obligatory upon the non-consummated woman. As for the consummated woman, she has 'iddah maintenance if he apostatized, like the mahr; and if she apostatized, she has the mahr but not maintenance. 'Iddah is obligatory upon her by menses, months, or delivery. Thus in al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'if she apostatizes' means the non-consummated woman; because separation came from her before the mahr was confirmed, so the mahr falls. [4] His statement: 'then both become Muslim' means together, either actually by both uttering the word of Islam together, or virtually by both becoming Muslim without knowing which preceded. The analogy (qiyas) would require that the marriage be void because apostasy contradicts marriage; but we rule that it is not void by istihsan. Evidence for this is the report that Banu Hanifah apostatized and then became Muslim in the time of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him, and they were not commanded to renew their marriages.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on al-Wiqayah covering the effect of apostasy by one or both spouses on the marriage, mahr, and 'iddah. The matn states that apostasy by either spouse is an annulment (faskh) that takes effect immediately without needing a qadi's verdict and without waiting for 'iddah (for a consummated wife). The commentator clarifies that this is Abu Hanifa's position, and also the view of Abu Yusuf. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan holds that the husband's apostasy is a talaq (revocable divorce), while the wife's apostasy is faskh, drawing analogy to the case of refusing Islam (al-iba' 'an al-Islam). Abu Hanifa distinguishes: apostasy is inherently contradictory to marriage (munafin), whereas talaq is a lifting (rafi') that does not contradict the marriage contract itself. Therefore, apostasy cannot be talaq. The matn then details mahr consequences: if the husband apostatizes after consummation, the wife gets full mahr; if before consummation, she gets half. If the wife apostatizes before consummation, she gets nothing; after consummation, she gets full mahr. The 'iddah maintenance follows similar logic. Finally, the passage addresses the case where both apostatize and then both return to Islam together: the marriage remains valid by istihsan, even though qiyas would dictate it is void. The commentator cites the historical incident of Banu Hanifah who apostatized en masse and later returned to Islam without being ordered to renew their marriages.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the marriage contract, distinguished from talaq in that it is not a divorce but a dissolution due to a defect or contradiction.
عاجل (ʿajil): Immediate effect, without waiting for judicial decree or 'iddah to elapse.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from qiyas in favor of a more equitable outcome, supported by precedent or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wife is entitled to full mahr
B) The wife is entitled to half the mahr
C) The marriage is suspended until he returns to Islam
D) The wife is entitled to nothing

Key Takeaway
Apostasy by a spouse is an immediate annulment (faskh) that terminates the marriage, but if both return to Islam together, the marriage remains valid by istihsan.

Rulings on Apostasy and Marriage: Immediate Dissolution and Mahr
Ruling statement with commentary on apostasy's immediate effect and mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of mahr when apostasy occurs, distinguishing between cohabited and non-cohabited wives, and explain the immediate effect of apostasy on marriage.

Core Idea
Apostasy instantly dissolves marriage; mahr depends on cohabitation and who apostatized.

This passage from al-Wiqayah and its commentary presents the core rulings on how apostasy affects marriage and mahr. The marriage dissolves immediately without judicial process, and the mahr entitlement varies based on whether the wife was cohabited and whether the husband or wife apostatized. Additionally, if both spouses apostatize together and then return to Islam, the marriage remains valid by istihsan, based on the precedent of Banu Hanifah.

Guided Path
1
Identify mahr for husband's apostasy
For a cohabited wife, full mahr is due; for a non-cohabited wife, half mahr.
The matn states: 'للموطوءة كل مهرها، ولغيرها نصفه لو ارتد'.
2
Identify mahr for wife's apostasy
If she apostatizes, she gets nothing if non-cohabited; if cohabited, she gets the mahr but not nafaqah during iddah.
The matn says 'ولا شيء لو ارتدت', and commentary [3] explains the reason.
3
Understand 'عاجل' (immediate)
The dissolution occurs without waiting for a judge's ruling or the expiry of iddah.
Commentary [1] clarifies: 'بلا توقف على قضاء القاضي، ولا توقف على مضي عدة'.
4
Apply istihsan for joint apostasy
If both apostatize simultaneously (literally or juridically) and then both return to Islam, the marriage remains valid, contrary to qiyas.
The precedent of Banu Hanifah at the time of Abu Bakr is cited as evidence.

Immediately, then for the one who was cohabited her full mahr, and for the other half of it if he apostatizes, and nothing if she apostatizes. And the marriage remains if both apostatized together, then they both re-embraced Islam, and it becomes invalid if one of them embraced Islam before the other. [1] His statement: 'Immediately' means without waiting for a judge's ruling, and without waiting for the expiry of iddah for the one cohabited. This is all according to him (Abu Hanifa) and according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, his apostasy is talaq and her apostasy is faskh, by analogy to the issue of refusal to accept Islam. And the distinction for Abu Hanifa is that apostasy is contradictory to marriage, while talaq lifts marriage without contradiction, so it is impossible to make it talaq unlike the case of refusal. Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of apostasy on marriage. It begins with the matn stating that apostasy has immediate effect without judicial process. For a cohabited wife, full mahr is due if the husband apostatizes; for a non-cohabited wife, half mahr. If the wife apostatizes, no mahr. If both apostatize together and then both re-embrace Islam, the marriage remains valid by istihsan (juridical preference), based on the precedent of Banu Hanifah. If one converts before the other, the marriage becomes invalid. The commentary then explains the disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that apostasy dissolves the marriage immediately (faskh), while Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes: the husband's apostasy is a talaq (revocable divorce), the wife's apostasy is faskh. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that apostasy is inherently contradictory to the marriage contract, whereas talaq is a lifting of the contract without contradiction. Therefore, apostasy cannot be classified as talaq. The commentary also clarifies mahr and iddah details: non-cohabited wife gets no mahr if she apostatizes because the separation came before the mahr was confirmed; the cohabited wife gets full mahr but not nafaqah if she apostatizes.

Key Terms
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy from Islam; the act of a Muslim renouncing the faith.
عاجل (ʿājil): Immediate; without delay or waiting for judicial process or iddah expiry.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment or dissolution of a contract without it being a divorce.
المهر (al-mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife as part of the marriage contract.
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): Juridical preference; setting aside qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Apostasy immediately dissolves marriage; mahr rules depend on cohabitation and who apostatized.
